Judge's Gatekeeper and Prohibition of Bribery
Ruling on judge's gatekeeper and bribery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling regarding a judge's gatekeeper and explain why a judgment becomes unenforceable if the judge knows of bribery.

Core Idea
A gatekeeper cannot take payment for entry, and a judge aware of such payment cannot enforce that judgment.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the commentator explains the rules governing a judge's gatekeeper. While the judge may appoint a gatekeeper to manage the crowd, the gatekeeper is absolutely forbidden from accepting any payment for entry. Moreover, if the judge knows of such taking and still passes judgment, that judgment is void like a bribe-based judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
Determine the hukm for two acts: (1) the judge appointing a gatekeeper – permissible; (2) the gatekeeper taking payment – prohibited (haram).
2
Identify the consequence of bribery with judge's knowledge
If the gatekeeper takes something and the judge knows, then the judgment is like a bribe-based judgment and is not enforceable (la yanfudh).
Knowledge of the judge is a key condition.
3
Underlying principle
The ruling is based on the prohibition of bribery (rashwah), which corrupts the judicial process. Any taint of bribery invalidates the judgment.
4
Note the source authority
This ruling is from later Hanafi works (fatawa mutakhkhirin), specifically Sharh Adab al-Qada, and is agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يتخذ بواباً ليمنع الخصوم من الإزدحام ولا يباح للبواب أن يأخذ شيئاً على الإذن في الدخول، وإذا أخذ البواب شيئاً وعلم القاضي به فقضى كان كالقضاء بالرشوة لا ينفذ؛ كذا في شرح أدب القضاء.
And he may appoint a gatekeeper to prevent litigants from crowding, and it is not permissible for the gatekeeper to take anything for permission to enter. And if the gatekeeper takes something and the judge knows of it and then passes judgment, it is like judging with bribery and is not enforceable; as in Sharh Adab al-Qada.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding the judge's authority to appoint a gatekeeper and the prohibition of taking bribes. The judge may appoint a gatekeeper to control the crowd and prevent disorder. However, the gatekeeper is absolutely forbidden from accepting any payment or gift in exchange for allowing litigants to enter. If the gatekeeper does accept something and the judge is aware of it yet still proceeds to judge the case, the judgment is equivalent to a bribe-based judgment and is null and void; it cannot be executed. This is based on the principle that accepting any benefit in connection with judicial proceedings corrupts the process. The reference to Sharh Adab al-Qada indicates this ruling is from later Hanafi fiqh works.

Key Terms
بواب (bawwāb): A gatekeeper appointed by the judge to control entry and crowd of litigants
رشوة (rashwah): Bribery; any payment given to influence a judge's decision

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The judgment is valid but the gatekeeper is sinful.
B) The judgment is like bribery and not enforceable.
C) The judgment is suspended until the payment is returned.
D) The judge must recuse himself but the judgment stands.

Key Takeaway
A gatekeeper cannot accept payment, and a judgment tainted by bribery known to the judge is void.

Summoning an Absent Opponent: Procedure and Distances
Ruling on summoning absent opponent with sealed writ and criteria for near/far

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for summoning an absent defendant and apply the near/far criterion based on same-day return.

Core Idea
A plaintiff may bring a defendant without extra proof only if the defendant can return home the same day.

In Hanafi court procedure, when a plaintiff wishes to summon an absent opponent, the judge provides a sealed writ. The key question is whether the opponent is near or far. This lesson unpacks the rule: near means same-day return; far requires proof. We also explore Imam Muhammad's recommendation to mitigate hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural steps
Read the passage to list the steps: plaintiff comes → judge gives sealed writ → writ commands appearance → if near, plaintiff brings him → if far, plaintiff must provide proof of location.
2
Define the near/far criterion
The text states that 'near' means the defendant can return to his family on the same day. Anything beyond that is 'far' and requires the plaintiff to establish proof before the judge proceeds.
This criterion prevents baseless summons and protects absent defendants.
3
Analyze the burden of proof
For a distant defendant, the judge does not act on the plaintiff's mere claim; the plaintiff must present evidence (bayyinah) that the defendant is indeed at that location.
This safeguards against false accusations.
4
Appreciate Imam Muhammad's addition
Imam Muhammad recommends that the imam appoint judges in every district for distances less than the travel duration (safar) to avoid the hardship of opponents traveling far. This is an administrative measure to remove oppression.
This reflects the Hanafi concern for justice and convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا جاء رجل أراد إحضار خصمه الغائب دفع له طينة عليها ختم القاضي مكتوب فيها أجب خصمك إلى مجلس الحكم فإن كان في المصر أحضره أو قريباً منه، وإن كان بعيداً فالقاضي لا يعديه بمجرد قوله حتى يقيم البينة والفاصل بينهما أنه إن أمكنه أن يعود إلى أهله في ذلك اليوم فهو قريب وإلا فلا. وقال محمد: يجب على الإمام أن ينصب قضاة على الكور فيما دون مدة السفر احترازاً عن شقة الأعداء وهو إزالة العدوان.
And if a man comes who wants to bring his absent opponent, the judge gives him a seal (ṭīnah) with the judge's stamp, written in it: 'Answer your opponent in the court of judgment.' So if he is in the city or near it, he brings him. And if he is far, the judge does not proceed based on his mere statement until he establishes proof. And the distinction between them is that if it is possible for him to return to his family on that same day, then he is near; otherwise not. And Muhammad said: It is incumbent upon the imam to appoint judges in the districts (kurr) for distances less than the duration of travel (safar) to avoid the hardship of opponents, which is the removal of oppression.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage covers the procedure for summoning an absent defendant. A plaintiff who wishes to bring his absent opponent to court must first obtain a sealed writ (ṭīnah) from the judge, which bears the judge's stamp and commands the defendant to appear. If the defendant is within the same city or close enough to return home within the day, the plaintiff may bring him without further proof. But if the defendant is at a distance that would require an overnight stay (i.e., travel distance, safar), the judge does not accept the plaintiff's mere claim; the plaintiff must present evidence (bayyinah) that the defendant is indeed at that location. The criterion for 'near' is whether the opponent can return to his home on the same day. Imam Muhammad adds that the ruler should appoint multiple judges in districts so that litigants do not have to travel far, thus preventing oppression.

Key Terms
طينة (ṭīnah): A seal or stamped clay used as a writ for summons
شقة الأعداء (shuqqat al-aʿdāʾ): Hardship of opponents; difficulty faced by litigants in traveling to court

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for a defendant being considered 'near' such that the plaintiff can bring him without additional proof?
A) The defendant lives within the city limits.
B) The defendant can return to his family within the same day.
C) The distance is less than three days' travel.
D) The plaintiff knows the defendant's exact address.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi procedure, the nearness of a defendant is determined by same-day return, not distance, and distant defendants require proof from the plaintiff.

Sealing the House of a Defiant Defendant in Hanafi Qada
Ruling on sealing the house of a defiant defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sealing a defendant's house and identify the conditions and precedents that justify it.

Core Idea
Sealing a house to coerce a defendant is permissible, based on Umar's practice, not strict qiyas.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, when a defendant conceals himself in his house to avoid summons, the judge is authorized to seal the house, effectively making it a prison. This coercive measure is not based on strict analogy (qiyas) but on the practice of Caliph Umar (ra) and later righteous judges. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and important conditions, including how it applies to rented and shared properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify excuses for non-attendance
Recognize that opponents are excused from court due to illness or if the woman is secluded (mukhaddarah). These are legitimate reasons that prevent sealing.
The Hanafi school respects custom and modesty for secluded women.
2
State the ruling on concealment
If the defendant hides in his house, the judge may seal the house, block all exits, and make it a prison until he emerges. The method does not involve storming (hujum) but sending women to request him and guards to secure the perimeter.
Al-Halwani reports that the Hanafi school did not permit storming.
3
Identify the legal proof (dalil)
The ruling is based on the practice of Caliph Umar and the righteous after him. This is a qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion) that justifies departing from strict qiyas.
The dalil is zanni in thubut and dalala but carries authority in judicial practice.
4
Apply nuances: rented vs. shared house
For a rented house, there is disagreement about nailing the door shut; the preferred view is that it is allowed. For a shared house, nailing for one co-owner is considered unjust; other co-owners may request removal of nails.
The judge must act justly and not harm innocent co-owners.
5
Note additional authorities and costs
The sultan may seal the debtor's door even without concealment to pressure payment. Assistants' wages are from the treasury if the defendant is rebellious; otherwise the plaintiff bears the cost of the agent.
This reflects later Hanafi practice (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسقط الأعداء بعذر المرض أو كانت مخدرة، فإن توارى الخصم في بيته ختم القاضي على بيته وجعل بيته عليه سجناً وسد أعلاه وأسفله حتى يضيق عليه الأمر فيخرج قال الحلواني : وأصحابنا لم يجوزوا الهجوم وصورته أن يبعث القاضي نساء يطلبنه في البيت وأعواناً يأخذون السفل والعلو كيلا يهرب. وهذا هو القياس فعله عمر رضي الله عنه والصالحون من بعده وتركوا فيه القياس . فإن كان المديون يسكن داراً بأجرة وامتنع من الحضور اختلفوا في تسمير الباب، والأصح أنه يسمر. والتسمير الضرب بالمسامير .اهـ. فإن كانت الدار مشتركة فسمرها الحاكم لأجل أحد الشركاء للباقي أن يرفعوا الأمر إليه ليرفع المسامير وليس هذا. من العدل؛ كذا في التتارخانية. وفيها : للسلطان الختم على باب المديون وإن لم يتوار في بيته تضييقاً عليه حتى يقضي الدين اهـ. فعلى هذا له وضعه في الجاويش في زماننا وفي البزازية : ويستعين بأعوان الوالي على الإحضار وأجرة الأشخاص في بيت المال، وقيل على المتمرد في المصر من : درهم إلى درهم، وفي الخارج لكل فرسخ ثلاثة دراهم أو أربعة وأجرة الموكل على المدعي نصف
And opponents are excused due to illness or if (the woman) is secluded (mukhaddarah). Then if the opponent conceals himself in his house, the judge seals his house and makes his house a prison for him, and blocks its top and bottom until the matter becomes tight on him and he comes out. Al-Halwani said: Our companions did not permit storming (al-hujūm), and its form is that the judge sends women to request him in the house and assistants to take the bottom and top so that he does not flee. And this is the qiyas (analogy) – 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, and the righteous after him did it, and they abandoned the qiyas therein. Then if the debtor lives in a rented house and refuses to attend, there is disagreement about nailing the door, and the most correct is that it is nailed. And al-tasmīr is striking with nails. If the house is shared, and the judge nailed it for one of the partners, the rest may raise the matter to him to remove the nails, and this is not justice; as in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And in it: The sultan may seal the door of the debtor even if he did not conceal himself in his house, to pressure him until he pays the debt. Upon this, he may put him in the prison (al-jāwīsh) in our time. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: He seeks help from the assistants of the governor for bringing (the defendant), and the wages of the persons are from the treasury (bayt al-māl), and it is said that on the rebellious (al-mutamarrid) in the city: from one dirham to one dirham, and outside for each farsakh three or four dirhams, and the wage of the agent (al-muwakkal) is on the plaintiff: half.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage deals with methods to compel a defiant defendant to appear in court. It starts by noting that certain excuses (illness, seclusion of women) justify non-attendance. If the defendant hides in his house, the judge may seal the house effectively making it a prison, blocking all exits until he is forced out. Al-Halwani reports that the Hanafi school does not permit storming the house (hujūm); instead, the judge sends women to request him and assistants to guard the perimeter. The commentator notes that this practice (sealing) is contrary to qiyas (analogy) but was done by Caliph 'Umar and later righteous judges, thus becoming a legitimate exception. For a rented house, there is disagreement on nailing the door shut; the preferred view is that it is allowed to nail it. If the house is co-owned, nailing for one co-owner's debt is considered unjust and the other co-owners may ask the judge to remove the nails. Additionally, the sultan may seal the debtor's door even without concealment to pressure payment. In the author's time, this included imprisonment (jāwīsh). The assistants' wages are from the public treasury, but if the defendant is rebellious, he may be charged a fee. The plaintiff bears the cost of the agent (muwakkal) initially.

Key Terms
تسمير (tasmīr): Nailing the door shut to prevent escape
المخدرة (al-mukhaddarah): A woman who is secluded and does not go out
الهجوم (al-hujūm): Storming the house by force

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permitted if the judge orders it.
B) The Hanafi school did not permit storming; instead send women and guards.
C) Storming is the preferred method.
D) Storming is only allowed for rented houses.

Key Takeaway
Sealing a defendant's house is a permissible coercive measure in Hanafi qada, based on the precedent of Umar and conditioned by the absence of excuses and justice to co-owners.

Fees for Court Personnel and Sealing Doors
Rulings on fees for summoning officers and court decorum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on permissible fees for court ushers, agents, and the conditions for sealing a defendant's house.

Core Idea
Court personnel may take limited fees; sealing a defendant's door requires proof and no excuse.

The judge needs assistance to summon defendants. This passage outlines who pays the fees (treasury or rebellious defendant), limits on amounts, and when a judge may seal a defendant's house. The commentary by al-Ramli stresses that such actions require evidence and are not to be done arbitrarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify different court personnel and their services
The passage mentions 'a'wān al-wālī' (assistants of the governor), 'jaluwāz' (court usher), and 'muwakkal' (agent). Note their roles: assistants bring defendants, usher manages seating and decorum, agent is appointed by plaintiff to fetch defendant.
2
Determine who bears the cost
In normal cases, wages are from the treasury (bayt al-māl). If the defendant is rebellious, he may be charged. The agent's fee is on the plaintiff.
Some say fee on rebellious defendant, others on plaintiff; no final resolution.
3
Identify fee limits
For rebellious defendant: in city about 1 dirham, outside per farsakh 3 or 4 dirhams. For court usher: maximum 2 dirhams from plaintiff.
The jaluwāz's fee is capped to avoid exploitation.
4
Analyze procedure for sealing a door
Before sealing, the judge must require the plaintiff to establish proof that the defendant is at home and has no valid excuse (e.g., illness, travel). This ruling is from al-Ramli, citing al-Khaniyyah and al-Tatarkhaniyyah.
Sealing is a coercive measure; due process is required.
5
Note the warning against arbitrary actions
The passage cautions judges against acting without proper evidence, especially when the defendant may have an excuse that permits even missing Friday prayer.
The principle of caution and justice is emphasized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستعين بأعوان الوالي على الإحضار وأجرة الأشخاص في بيت المال، وقيل على المتمرد في المصر من : درهم إلى درهم، وفي الخارج لكل فرسخ ثلاثة دراهم أو أربعة وأجرة الموكل على المدعي نصف فإن توارى الخصم في بيته ختم القاضي على بابه قال الرملي بعد أن يكلف القاضي المدعي إلى إقامته البينة أنه في منزله كما صرح به في الخانية والتتارخانية نقلاً عن المحيط، ومحل ذلك أيضاً إذا لم يكن له عذر كما صرح به علماء الشافعية وقواعدنا تقضي به أيضاً فاعلم ذلك ولا تغتر بما يفعله بعض القضاة فإن محل السمر والختم إذا ثبت امتناع الخصم بلا عذر ولو كان عذراً يبيح ترك صلاة الجمعة تأمل قوله: (وهذا هو القياس قال الرملي : اسم الإشارة راجع إلى قوله وأصحابنا لم يجوزوا الهجوم» تأمل قوله : ( وتركوا الخ) أي أصحاب نبينا قوله : ( وأجرة الأشخاص في بيت المال) قال في الحكام وفي القنية : وينبغي أن ينصب إنساناً حتى يقعد الناس بين يدي القاضي ويقيمهم ويقعد الشهود ويقيمهم ويزجر من يسيء الأب ويسمى صاحب المجلس والجلواز أيضاً، وأنه يأخذ من المدعي شيئاً لأنه يعمل له بإقعاد الشهود على الترتيب وغيره لكن لا يأخذ أكثر من در همين. وللوكلاء لسان
And he seeks help from the assistants of the governor for bringing (the defendant), and the wages of the persons are from the treasury, and it is said that on the rebellious in the city: from one dirham to one dirham, and outside for each farsakh three or four dirhams, and the wage of the agent is on the plaintiff: half. Then if the opponent conceals himself in his house, the judge seals his door. Al-Ramli said: after the judge requires the plaintiff to establish proof that he is in his home, as stated in al-Khaniyyah and al-Tatarkhaniyyah, quoting from al-Muhit. And this is also if he has no excuse, as stated by Shafi'i scholars and our principles also require it. So know that and do not be deceived by what some judges do, for the place of nailing and sealing is when the opponent's refusal without excuse is established, even if there is an excuse that permits leaving Friday prayer. Consider his statement: 'This is the qiyas.' Al-Ramli said: The demonstrative pronoun refers to his statement 'Our companions did not permit storming'... Consider his statement: 'And they left...' meaning the companions of our Prophet. His statement: 'And the wages of the persons are from the treasury.' He said in al-Hukkam and al-Qunyah: It is recommended to appoint a person to seat people before the judge, stand them up, seat witnesses, stand them up, and rebuke whoever misbehaves. He is called 'ṣāḥib al-majlis' and also 'jaluwāz'. And he may take something from the plaintiff because he works for him by seating witnesses in order etc., but he should not take more than two dirhams. And for the agents (wukalāʾ) [they take] a tongue.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage deals with the costs associated with court proceedings, specifically the fees for summoning officers and other personnel. The judge may enlist the help of the governor's assistants to bring a defendant. Their wages are normally paid from the public treasury (bayt al-māl). However, if the defendant is rebellious (mutamarrid), some say they can charge him a fee: in the city about one dirham, and outside the city for each farsakh (approximately 5.5 km) three or four dirhams. The plaintiff initially bears the cost of the agent (muwakkal) — half dirham etc. On the issue of sealing the house, al-Ramli clarifies that before sealing, the judge must require the plaintiff to prove that the defendant is indeed at home and has no valid excuse. This ruling is also in agreement with Shafi'i principles. The passage warns against judges who act without proper evidence. The detailed commentary from al-Hukkam and al-Qunyah explains that a person should be appointed to manage the court session (ṣāḥib al-majlis or jaluwāz), who organizes the seating of parties and witnesses and maintains decorum. This person may receive a small fee from the plaintiff (up to two dirhams) for his services. The agents (wukalāʾ) also have prescribed fees, though the text cuts off.

Key Terms
جلواز (jaluwāz): Court usher or officer who manages the session and seats people
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance; approximately 5.5 km or 3 miles
موكل (muwakkal): Agent appointed by the plaintiff to bring the defendant

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One dirham.
B) Two dirhams.
C) Three dirhams.
D) Five dirhams.

Key Takeaway
Court personnel fees are regulated and limited; coercive measures like sealing doors require strong evidence.

Punishment for Contempt and Courtroom Procedure
Ruling on disciplining a defendant and court administration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on punishment for contempt, identify when a judge may accept written petitions, and list the conditions for appointing a court scribe.

Core Idea
Contempt requires proof; written petitions are barred during court sessions; scribes must be trustworthy.

A judge's authority includes disciplining defiant litigants, but only after clear evidence. The Hanafi school also strictly limits the use of written submissions to preserve oral testimony and sets high standards for court scribes to ensure integrity. This lesson unpacks these interconnected procedural rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge punishing a defendant who defiantly refuses to attend court. The ruling is that the judge may punish (taʿzīr) according to the degree of defiance.
No specific punishment is fixed; it is discretionary.
2
Identify the condition for punishment
The defiance (tamarrud) must be established (thabata). Mere allegation is insufficient; proof is required before any punishment.
Proof could be testimony of the summoning officer or other witnesses.
3
Examine the rule on written petitions (qisas)
The Hanafi madhhab: when the judge is sitting for judgment (julūs li-l-qaḍāʾ), he does not accept written petitions. Outside of court sessions, he may accept them. Also, the judge is not held responsible for content unless the litigant explicitly affirms it orally.
The ikhtilaf mentioned by al-Sadr al-Shahid shows there was a scholarly difference.
4
List the conditions for appointing a scribe
The scribe must be righteous, chaste, and qualified to give testimony. Excluded are dhimmis, slaves, children, and those whose testimony is invalid. The scribe records the proceedings and files them in a folder (qimṭar), one per month.
This ensures accurate record keeping and accountability.
5
Apply the rulings to a practical scenario
Consider a defendant who repeatedly ignores court summons without excuse. The judge must first verify the defiance (e.g., through the bailiff's report), then impose proportionate discipline. Meanwhile, if a litigant hands a written petition during the hearing, the judge should refuse to read it.
This illustrates the integration of all three sub-rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت تمرده عن الحضور عاقبه بقدره. وذكر الصدر الشهيد لاختلاف في قبول القاضي القصص من الخصوم والمذهب عندنا أنه لا يأخذها إذا جلس للقضاء وإلا أخذها . ثم ذكر الاختلاف في أن القاضي يؤاخذ بما كتب فيها والمذهب لا إلا إذا أقر بلفظه صريحاً. وفي السراج الوهاج : وينبغي للقاضي أن يتخذ كاتباً صالحاً عفيفاً ويقعده بحيث يراه أهلاً للشهادة لا ذمياً ولا عبداً ولا صبياً ولا ممن لا تجوز شهادته فيكتب الخصومة وبجعلها في فمطره ويجعل لكل شهر فمطراً.
And when his defiance of attendance is established, he punishes him according to (the extent of his defiance). And al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned a difference regarding the judge accepting written petitions (qisas) from litigants; the madhhab with us is that he does not take them when he sits for judgment, otherwise he takes them. Then he mentioned a difference regarding whether the judge is held accountable for what is written in them; the madhhab is no, unless he admits it explicitly with his own words. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: It is recommended for the judge to appoint a righteous, chaste scribe (kātib) and seat him where he considers him fit for testimony — not a dhimmi, nor a slave, nor a child, nor anyone whose testimony is not permissible — and he writes the lawsuit and puts it in his folder (qimṭar) and for each month he makes a folder.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage covers two related topics: punishment for contempt of court and appointment of a scribe. First, once the defendant's defiance (tamarrud) in attending court is proven by clear evidence (not mere allegation), the judge may punish him in proportion to his defiance. This punishment can include beating, imprisonment, or other disciplinary measures as the judge sees fit. The text notes that a mere report from the summoning officer is not enough; proof is required. This aligns with Hanafi principles that punishment requires established evidence. Second, regarding written petitions (qisas) — some early scholars differed on whether a judge should accept written submissions. The Hanafi madhhab holds that the judge should not accept written petitions once he has taken his seat for judgment; he should hear oral testimony. However, outside of court sessions, he may accept them. Also, the judge is not held responsible for the content of written petitions unless the litigant explicitly affirms the content orally in court. Third, the passage from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj recommends that the judge appoint a righteous and chaste scribe. The scribe must be someone whose testimony is accepted in court, excluding dhimmis, slaves, children, and others whose testimony is invalid. The scribe records the proceedings and keeps them in a folder (qimṭar), with a separate folder for each month.

Key Terms
تمرد (tamarrud): Defiance; willful refusal to attend court despite valid summons
قصص (qisas (here: written petitions)): Written statements or petitions submitted to the judge
قمطر (qimṭar): A folder or bag for storing court documents

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) At any time.
B) Only when he is not sitting for judgment (i.e., outside court session).
C) Never.
D) Only after the litigant pays a fee.

Key Takeaway
The judge must ensure contempt is proven before punishment, adhere to procedural rules (no written petitions during sessions), and appoint a qualified scribe for proper record keeping.

Gifts to the Judge: General Rule and Exceptions
Ruling on judge accepting gifts with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule on judges accepting gifts, identify the two exceptions (close relative and habitual giver), and explain the legal reasoning from the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya.

Core Idea
A judge must reject all gifts unless from a close relative or a pre-appointment habit.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical issue in judicial ethics: can a judge accept gifts? The general principle is a clear prohibition based on the Prophet's ﷺ rebuke of his official Ibn al-Lutbiyya. However, the Hanafi school recognizes two exceptions: gifts from close relatives, and gifts from those who customarily gave before the judge's appointment. Understanding these exceptions requires careful analysis of the intent behind the gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is the default rule for judges accepting gifts? The text states: 'He returns a gift (yaruddu hadiyyah)' – the judge must reject gifts.
This is the general principle, derived from the hadith.
2
List the exceptions
From whom may the judge accept? The text says: 'except from a relative (qarīb) or from one whose habit it is (man jarat ʿādatuhu bihi).' Identify both exceptions.
These exceptions are based on the unlikelihood of bias.
3
Locate the dalil
Which hadith is cited and what is its context? The hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, narrated by al-Bukhari from Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi. The Prophet appointed him as a sadaqah collector; when he returned, he distinguished the collected sadaqah from a personal gift.
The proof is a hadith ahad, but its indication is clear.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the general prohibition? The Prophet's rebuke shows that the gift was received because of his office, making it illicit. The same applies to judges: any gift linked to their position is prohibited unless an exception applies.
The illa (effective cause) is the potential for bias due to the office.
5
Apply the exceptions
Why are relatives and habitual givers excepted? Gifts from relatives are customary and less likely to influence. Similarly, gifts from someone who habitually gave before appointment are not seen as bribes because the practice predates the judicial role.
The exceptions preserve the intent behind the prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويرد هدية إلا من قريب أو ممن جرت عادته به) أي لا يقبل القاضي هدية لما رواه البخاري عن أبي حميد الساعدي قال : استعمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجلا من الأزد يقال له ابن اللتبية على الصدقة واسمه عبد الله، فلما قدم قال هذا لكم هذا أهدي إلي . فقال عليه
His statement: (And he returns a gift except from a relative or from one whose habit it is) meaning: The judge should not accept a gift, due to what al-Bukhari narrated from Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi: The Prophet ﷺ appointed a man from Azd called Ibn al-Lutbiyya (his name 'Abdullah) over the charity (sadaqah). When he returned, he said: 'This is for you, and this was given to me as a gift.' He (the Prophet) said: ...
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on judges accepting gifts. The primary principle is that a judge must refuse all gifts because they may influence his judgment. However, there are two exceptions: (1) gifts from close relatives (qarīb), and (2) gifts from someone who customarily gave gifts to the judge before his appointment (man jarat ʿādatuhu bihi). The proof cited is the well-known hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, recorded by al-Bukhari. The Prophet ﷺ had appointed Ibn al-Lutbiyya as a collector of sadaqah. When he returned, he presented the collected sadaqah and said, 'This is for you (the state), and this (other thing) was given to me as a gift.' The Prophet ﷺ then rebuked him, making it clear that any gift received by a public official in connection with his office is considered illicit. The same principle applies to judges. The exceptions are based on the idea that gifts from relatives are not likely to be bribes, as they are customary among kin. Similarly, if someone habitually gave gifts before the appointment, the gift is not seen as a bribe for a favorable ruling. The text is cut off, but the meaning is clear.

Key Terms
هدية (hadiyyah): A gift presented to the judge, generally prohibited
ابن اللتبية (Ibn al-Lutbiyya): A companion appointed as sadaqah collector; his case is a proof against accepting gifts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited for prohibiting judges from accepting gifts?
A) A verse from the Quran on bribery.
B) The hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya about gifts to officials.
C) Consensus of the companions.
D) A logical argument that gifts corrupt.

Key Takeaway
A judge must reject all gifts except those from close relatives or from those who customarily gave before appointment, based on the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya.

The Judge's Authority to Punish a Defiant Defendant and Court Procedures
Defines the judge's authority to punish a defiant defendant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the requirement of proof before punishing tamarrud, explain the discretionary nature of ta'zir, and describe the Hanafi rules regarding written petitions and court scribes.

Core Idea
A judge may punish a defiant defendant only after proof of defiance is established; the punishment is discretionary ta'zir.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the judge's authority to compel court attendance. It establishes that mere absence is insufficient; the judge must first prove the defendant's defiance (tamarrud). The passage also outlines the use of a personal agent (mushakhkhas), the conditions for seeking external help, and the proper handling of written petitions and scribes. These rulings ensure the integrity of the judicial process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for punishment
The text states 'إذا ثبت تمرده' (when his defiance is established). This means the judge cannot punish based on mere absence; clear proof (burhan) is required. Identify what constitutes proof in this context.
Proof can be witness testimony or the defendant's own admission of refusal.
2
Understand the discretionary punishment
The punishment is ta'zir, left to the judge's discretion 'بقدره' (according to its measure). Recognize that ta'zir is not fixed by law but varies with the severity of defiance.
Hanafi fiqh emphasizes proportionality in ta'zir.
3
Analyze the role of the personal agent
The text refers to 'المشخص' (al-mushakhkhas) – a person appointed to accompany the defendant and ensure attendance. This is a preparatory measure before punishment.
If the mushakhkhas fails, the judge may consider stronger measures.
4
Evaluate the option of seeking sultan's help
Some scholars allow seeking help from the sultan's officials first, but the fatwa (mufta bihi) is that this is only permissible if the judge is unable to enforce attendance directly.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of preserving judicial authority.
5
Assess the rules on written petitions and scribe
The text mentions that the madhhab holds the judge should not accept written petitions when sitting for judgment, and is not liable for their content unless he verbally confirms. The judge should appoint a righteous, qualified scribe to record proceedings.
These rules aim to prevent undue influence and ensure accurate records.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الأصح. وفي الذخيرة إنه المشخص وهو المأمور بملازمة المدعى عليه. وأطلق بعض المشايخ الذهاب إلى باب السلطان والاستعانة بأعوانه أوّلاً لاستيفاء حقه قبل العجز عن الاستيفاء بالقاضي لكنه لا يفتى به إلا إذا عجز القاضي. وإذا ثبت تمرده عن الحضور عاقبه بقدره. وذكر الصدر الشهيد لاختلاف في قبول القاضي القصص من الخصوم والمذهب عندنا أنه لا يأخذها إذا جلس للقضاء وإلا أخذها . ثم ذكر الاختلاف في أن القاضي يؤاخذ بما كتب فيها والمذهب لا إلا إذا أقر بلفظه صريحاً. وفي السراج الوهاج : وينبغي للقاضي أن يتخذ كاتباً صالحاً عفيفاً ويقعده بحيث يراه أهلاً للشهادة لا ذمياً ولا عبداً ولا صبياً ولا ممن لا تجوز شهادته فيكتب الخصومة وبجعلها في فمطره ويجعل لكل شهر فمطراً.
And it is the most correct. In al-Dhakhira: he is the personal agent who is ordered to accompany the defendant. Some scholars permit going to the door of the sultan and seeking help from his assistants first to obtain his right before being unable to obtain it through the judge, but it is not given as a fatwa unless the judge is unable. And when his defiance regarding attendance is established, the judge punishes him according to its measure. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned a difference regarding the judge accepting petitions from litigants; the madhhab according to us is that he does not take them when he sits for judgment, otherwise he takes them. Then he mentioned the difference regarding whether the judge is held liable for what is written in them; the madhhab is no, unless he admits orally explicitly. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: it is befitting for the judge to appoint a righteous, chaste scribe and seat him in a place where he considers him qualified for testimony – not a dhimmi, a slave, a child, or one whose testimony is not permissible – so that he writes the litigation and places it in his basket, and makes for each month a basket.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling on the judge's response to a defendant who refuses to appear in court. The text establishes that the judge may punish such a defendant only after defiance is proven. The punishment is discretionary (ta'zir) and proportional. The earlier part discusses the role of a 'مشخص' (personal agent) appointed to accompany the defendant and ensure attendance. Some scholars permit seeking help from the sultan's officials first, but the relied-upon position is that this is only when the judge is unable to enforce attendance. The discussion then shifts to the judge's acceptance of written petitions (قصص): the Hanafi position is that the judge should not accept them when sitting for judgment, but may accept them otherwise. The judge is not liable for their content unless he explicitly verbally confirms it. Finally, the passage recommends that the judge appoint a righteous and reliable scribe, who should be free, adult, and trustworthy, to record the proceedings and store them in a basket (قمطر) for each month.

Key Terms
مشخص (mushakhkhas): A person appointed by the judge to accompany the defendant and ensure his attendance at court.
تمرد (tamarrud): Defiance or refusal of the defendant to appear in court after being summoned.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment left to the judge's judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A judge may punish a defiant defendant only after proof of defiance; punishment is discretionary, and ancillary procedures like written petitions and scribes must follow Hanafi rules.

The Judge's Clerk and Court Fees in Hanafi Law
Details the appointment of a righteous scribe and payment of fees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the qualifications of a court scribe according to the Hanafi school and identify who bears the costs of different court officials.

Core Idea
Court fees are assigned to incentivize truthful litigation, with the plaintiff bearing initial costs.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details the administrative setup of a Shari'a court: the scribe must be trustworthy and qualified, and the fees for messengers and other staff are regulated to ensure justice and deter frivolous claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scribe's qualifications
From the source, list the conditions that the scribe must meet: righteousness, chastity, and being fit for testimony (not a dhimmi, slave, child, or anyone whose testimony is invalid).
The scribe's integrity is crucial for accurate record-keeping.
2
Determine who pays the scribe and deputies
The scribe's wage is on the person who requests the document (typically the plaintiff). The deputies (nuwwab) are paid by the judge from the public treasury.
The judge does not personally pay the scribe.
3
Analyze the fee structure for foot messengers
Foot messengers (rajjala) are paid by the plaintiff: in the city, 0.5 to 1 dirham; outside, 3-4 dirhams per farsakh. These rates were set by past pious scholars.
The amounts are reasonable and based on custom.
4
Understand the istihsan rule for messenger fees
Initially, the messenger's fee is on the plaintiff. If the plaintiff refuses to pay, it shifts to the defendant as a deterrent to frivolous claims.
This istihsan overrides the qiyas that would keep the fee on the plaintiff always.
5
Relate to the principle of deterrence
The shifting fee encourages plaintiffs to bring only serious cases and discourages harassment of defendants through litigation costs.
The rule balances access to justice with prevention of abuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج الوهاج : وينبغي للقاضي أن يتخذ كاتباً صالحاً عفيفاً ويقعده بحيث يراه أهلاً للشهادة لا ذمياً ولا عبداً ولا صبياً ولا ممن لا تجوز شهادته فيكتب الخصومة وبجعلها في فمطره ويجعل لكل شهر فمطراً. أن يأخذوا ممن يعملون لهم من المدعين والمدعى عليهم ولكن لا يأخذوا والكل مجلس أكثر من در همين. والرجالة يأخذون أجورهم ممن يعملون له وهم المدعون لكنهم يأخذون في المصر نصف درهم إلى درهم وإذا خرجوا إلى الرساتيق لا يأخذون لكل فرسخ أكثر من ثلاثة دراهم أو أربعة؛ هكذا وضعه العلماء الأتقياء الكبار وهي أجور أمثالهم. وأجرة الكاتب على من يكتب له الكتابة، وأجرة النواب على القاضي، وإذا بعث أميناً للتعديل فالجعل على المدعي كالصحيفة. قال مجد الأئمة التركماني : مؤنة الرجالة على المدعي في الابتداء فإذا امتنع فعلى المدعى عليه وكان ذلك استحساناً مال إليه للزجر فإن القياس أن يكون على المدعي في الحالين المزكي يأخذ الأجر من المدعي وكذا المبعوث للتعديل .
In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: It is befitting for the judge to take a righteous, chaste scribe and seat him in a place where he sees him fit for testimony – not a dhimmi, a slave, a child, or one whose testimony is not permissible – so he writes the litigation and places it in his basket, and makes for each month a basket. That they take from those for whom they work, from the plaintiffs and defendants, but they should not take, and all council not more than two dirhams. The foot messengers take their wages from the one for whom they work, and they are the plaintiffs, but in the city they take half a dirham to a dirham, and when they go out to the farmlands, they do not take for each farsakh more than three or four dirhams; thus the great pious scholars set it, and these are their like wages. The scribe's wage is on the one for whom he writes the document, and the wage of the deputies is on the judge. And if he sends a trustee for ta'dil, the fee is on the plaintiff like the document. Majd al-A'imma al-Turkumani said: The expense of the foot messengers is on the plaintiff initially, and if he refuses, then on the defendant, and this is istihsan inclined to for deterrence, for the qiyas would be that it is on the plaintiff in both cases. The one who pays the zakat takes the wage from the plaintiff, and likewise the one sent for ta'dil.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage provides guidelines for the administrative staff of the judge. The judge should appoint a scribe (كاتب) who is righteous, chaste, and qualified to be a witness – not a dhimmi, slave, child, or anyone whose testimony is invalid. The scribe records the proceedings and stores them in a basket (قمطر), organizing them monthly. The fees for court officials are specified: the scribe is paid by the person who requests the writing (typically the plaintiff). The foot messengers (رجالة) who summon parties are paid by the one who employs them, i.e., the plaintiff, with a set rate: in the city between half and one dirham, and outside the city per farsakh (about 3 miles) three or four dirhams. The deputies (نواب) are paid by the judge from the treasury. If the judge appoints a trustee to verify witnesses (تعديل), the fee is on the plaintiff, like for the document. There is an istihsan: initially the expense of messengers is on the plaintiff; if he refuses, it shifts to the defendant, as a deterrent to frivolous claims. By qiyas, it would always be on the plaintiff.

Key Terms
كاتب (katib): Court scribe who records litigations and organizes documents.
رجالة (rajjala): Foot messengers who serve summons and other court errands.
تعديل (ta'dil): Process of verifying the credibility of witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who initially bears the cost of the foot messenger (رجالة) according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) The judge
B) The defendant
C) The plaintiff
D) The public treasury

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school assigns court fees to the litigants, with an istihsan shifting the messenger's cost to the defendant if the plaintiff refuses, to deter frivolous claims.

The Prohibition of Gifts to Judges: Proof and Ruling
Prohibits judges from accepting gifts except from relatives or customary givers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on gifts to judges and explain the prophetic proof (hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya) that establishes the prohibition.

Core Idea
A gift given because of a judicial position is a bribe in substance; the Prophet's reprimand proves it is haram.

The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that a judge must return gifts except from relatives or customary givers. The commentary expands on the prohibition with a hadith and the famous statement of Umar ibn Abdul Aziz. This lesson focuses on the general rule and its foundational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the judge accepting a gift. The ruling is haram (prohibited).
The matn says 'yaruddu al-hadiyah' (he returns the gift), implying prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The proof is the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya from Bukhari, where the Prophet criticized a charity collector for accepting gifts.
The hadith is quoted in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal implication)
The Prophet's rhetorical question 'Why did he not sit in his father's house...' indicates that the gift was given because of his position, making it prohibited.
Hanafi usul: strong censure implies prohibition (tahrim).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
The Shafi'i school also prohibits gifts to judges, but with nuances. The Hanafi position is clear and relied upon.
Umar ibn Abdul Aziz said: 'Gifts then were gifts, today they are bribes.'
5
Evaluate the authority
The hadith is sahih (zanni al-thubut) but its implication (dalala) is qati (definitive) in this context. The position is from zahir al-riwayah.
The ruling is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويرد هدية إلا من قريب أو ممن جرت عادته به) أي لا يقبل القاضي هدية لما رواه البخاري عن أبي حميد الساعدي قال : استعمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجلا من الأزد يقال له ابن اللتبية على الصدقة واسمه عبد الله، فلما قدم قال هذا لكم هذا أهدي إلي . فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام هلا جلس في بيت أبيه أو بيت أمه فينظر أيهدى إليه أم لا؟ قال عمر بن عبد العزيز رضي الله عنه : كانت الهدية على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هدية واليوم رشوة. فتعليله دليل على تحريم الهدية التي سببها الولاية ويجب ردها على صاحبها فإن تعذر ردها على مالكها وضعها في بيت المال كاللقطة كما في فتح القدير فإن كان المهدي يتأذى بالرد يقبلها ويعطيه مثل قيمتها؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفي المضمرات : إذا دخلت الهدية له من الباب خرجت الأمانة . من الكوة. وقدمنا عن الأقطع الفرق بين الهدية والرشوة أن الرشوة ما كان معها شرط الإعانة بخلاف الهدية. وفي خزانة المفتين : مال يعطيه ولا يكون معها شرط، والرشوة مال يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه. وذكر الهدية في الكتاب ليس احترازياً إذ يحرم عليه الاستقراض والاستعارة ممن يحرم عليه قبول هديته كما في الخانية. وإنما يقبل هدية القريب لما فيها من صلة الرحم وردها قطعية وهي حرام وأطلقه وهو مقيد بالمحرم فخرج ابن العم مثلاً، ومقيد بأن لا تكون له خصومة وإنما يقبل ممن له عادة للعلم بأنها ليست للقضاء وله شرطان: أن لا يكون له خصومة، وأن لا يزيد على العادة فيرد الكل في الأول وما زاد عليها في الثاني. وقيده فخر الإسلام بأن لا يكون مال المهدي قد زاد فبقدر ما زاد ماله لا بأس بقبوله. وظاهر العطف في كلام المصنف يقتضي أنه يقبل من القريب وإن لم تكن له عادة بالإهداء. وفي ما كلام بعضهم يقتضي أنه كالأجـ جنبي لا بد أن يكون له عادة وإلا فلا يقبلها منه إلا أن يكون لفقره ثم أيسر لأن الظاهر أن المانع ما كان إلا الفقر على وزان ما قاله فخر الإسلام في الزيادة والحاصل أن من له خصومة لا يقبلها مطلقاً، ومن لا خصومة له فإن كان له عادة قبل القضاء قبل المعتاد وإلا فلا. وفي تهذيب القلانسي : ولا يقبل هدية إلا من ذي رحم محرم أو من والي تولى الأمر منه أو وال مقدم الولاية على القضاء .اهـ. فعلى هذا له أن يقبلها السلطان من ومن حاكم بلده المسمى الآن بالباشاه واقتصر في التتارخانية على من وفي فتح القدير : وكل من عمل للمسلمين عملاً حكمه في الهدية حكم القاضي اهـ. فظاهره أنه يحرم قبولها على الوالي والمفتي وليس كما قال فقد قال في الخانية : ويجوز للإمام والمفتي قبول الهدية وإجابة الدعوة الخاصة لأن ذلك من حقوق المسلم على المسلم وإنما يمنع عنه القاضي .اهـ. إلا أن يراد بالإمام إمام الجامع. وفي التتارخانية من خصوصياته عليه الصلاة والسلام إن هداياه (له ذكر الخصوصية يفيد أنه ليس لإمام غيره
His saying: (And he returns a gift except from a relative or one with whom it is customary) i.e., the judge does not accept a gift because of what Bukhari narrated from Abu Hamid al-Sa'idi: The Prophet appointed a man from Azd called Ibn al-Lutbiyya over the charity, his name was Abdullah. When he came, he said: 'This is for you, this was given to me as a gift.' So he said: 'Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house and see if he would be given a gift or not?' Umar ibn Abdul Aziz said: Gifts in the time of the Prophet were gifts, today they are bribes. So his reasoning indicates the prohibition of a gift whose cause is the position, and it must be returned to its owner. If returning to its owner is impossible, it is placed in the treasury like a lost item as in Fath al-Qadir. But if the giver is hurt by the return, he accepts it and gives him its equivalent value; as in al-Khulasa. In al-Mudmarat: When the gift enters from the door, trust exits from the window. And we previously mentioned from al-Aqta' the difference between a gift and a bribe: a bribe is what has a condition of assistance, unlike a gift. In Khizanat al-Muftiyin: Wealth given without a condition, and a bribe is wealth given with the condition that he assist him. Mentioning a gift in the book is not restrictive, as it is also prohibited for him to borrow or seek a loan from those from whom accepting a gift is prohibited, as in al-Khaniyya. He only accepts the gift of a relative because of maintaining family ties; returning it severs relations, which is prohibited. It is unrestricted but restricted to a mahram relative, so paternal cousin is excluded. And restricted by his having no litigation. He only accepts from one with custom, because it is known it is not for the court. There are two conditions: that he have no litigation, and that it not exceed the custom. In the first case, he refuses all; in the second, what exceeds. Fakhr al-Islam restricted it: that the wealth of the giver has not increased; as much as his wealth increased, there is no problem accepting it. The apparent conjunction in the author's words implies that he accepts from a relative even if he has no custom of giving. And the words of some imply that he is like a stranger, must have a custom; otherwise he does not accept unless he was poor and then became rich, because the apparent prevention was only poverty, on the scale of what Fakhr al-Islam said about increase. The conclusion: one with litigation is absolutely not accepted; one without litigation: if he has custom before the post, he accepts the customary amount, otherwise no. In Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: He does not accept a gift except from a mahram relative or from a ruler under whose authority he is, or a governor preceding the ruler over the judiciary. Based on this, he may accept from the sultan and from the ruler of his town now called 'Pasha'. Al-Tatar Khaniyya restricted it to one who appointed him. In Fath al-Qadir: Everyone who works for the Muslims in a capacity has the same ruling as the judge regarding gifts. So apparently it is prohibited for the governor and mufti to accept, but it is not as he said; for al-Khaniyya states: It is permissible for the imam and mufti to accept gifts and respond to private invitations because that is among the rights of a Muslim to another Muslim; it is only prohibited for the judge. Unless by 'imam' is meant the congregational imam. In al-Tatar Khaniyya from his special characteristics, peace be upon him: his gifts...
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on gifts to judges. The matn states the judge must return gifts except from a relative or someone with a prior custom. The commentary provides proof from the hadith of Ibn al-Lutbiyya, where the Prophet criticized the official for accepting gifts, establishing that gifts given due to one's position are effectively bribes. Umar ibn Abdul Aziz famously said gifts in the Prophet's time were genuine, but now they are bribes. The ruling: such gifts are haram and must be returned. If return is impossible, they go to the public treasury. If the giver would be offended, the judge may accept but must give an equivalent value. The text distinguishes a gift (no condition of assistance) from a bribe (condition of assistance). The judge may accept gifts only from a mahram relative (excluding paternal cousins) or from someone who customarily gave before his appointment, provided the giver has no current litigation and the gift does not exceed the customary amount. If the giver's wealth recently increased, the judge may accept up to the increase. The passage also addresses whether rulers and muftis can accept gifts: the correct Hanafi view, per al-Khaniyya, is that they may, as it is a Muslim's right, unlike the judge. However, some extend the prohibition to all who work for Muslims. The Prophet's gifts were special and not for others.

Key Terms
هدية (hadiyah): A gift given to the judge without any condition of assistance.
رشوة (rashwah): A bribe given with a condition of assistance in the court.
محرم (mahram): A relative permanently prohibited for marriage, e.g., father, brother, uncle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the evidence that gifts to judges are haram in the Hanafi school?
A) The hadith where the Prophet criticized Ibn al-Lutbiyya for accepting gifts due to his position.
B) The Quranic verse on bribery.
C) Ijma of the companions.
D) Qiyas on theft.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits judges from accepting gifts because of the prophetic hadith and the principle that such gifts compromise justice; exceptions are only for mahram relatives and prior customary givers without litigation.

Exceptions to the Prohibition: Gifts from Relatives and Customary Givers
Details exceptions for relatives and customary givers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions under which a judge may accept gifts from relatives and customary givers, and identify when the gift must be refused.

Core Idea
A judge may only accept gifts from mahram relatives without litigation and from customary givers within customary limits.

While gifts to judges are generally haram, the Hanafi school carves out two exceptions: gifts from mahram relatives (to maintain family ties) and gifts from those who customarily gave before the judge's appointment (indicating the gift is not due to the position). This lesson explores the detailed conditions, including the prohibition if the giver has litigation, the restriction to mahram, and the handling of excess over custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exceptions
The judge may accept from a relative (mahram) or from a customary giver.
The matn says 'illa min qareeb aw mimman jarat 'adatuhu bihi'.
2
State the conditions for relatives
The relative must be mahram (excluding paternal cousin), and the relative must have no litigation with the judge.
The text says 'muqayyad bi al-mahram' and 'muqayyad bi an la takuna lahu khusuma'.
3
State the conditions for customary givers
The giver must have a prior habit of giving before the judge's appointment, no litigation, and the gift must not exceed the customary amount.
The text says 'wa lahu sharataan: an la takuna lahu khusuma, wa an la yazida 'ala al-'adah'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Fakhr al-Islam allows accepting if the giver's wealth recently increased. Some require custom even for relatives.
This shows the school's flexibility, but the mufta bihi is the standard position.
5
Apply to scenarios
Work through examples: a brother (mahram) without litigation – allowed; a paternal cousin (not mahram) – prohibited; a neighbour with prior custom but now a litigant – prohibited.
Litigation overrides both exceptions.

Source Text

Gifts for Judges: Distinguishing Gift from Bribe
Explains when a judge may accept gifts, distinguishing from bribery, and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when a judge may accept a gift and apply the distinction between gift and bribe to identify permissible and impermissible scenarios.

Core Idea
A gift to a judge becomes a bribe if given because of his position; permissible only from mahram or by prior custom.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles the critical issue of judges accepting gifts. It clarifies that the key factor is the motive and condition, not the gift itself. You will learn the precise legal distinction between a permissible gift (هدية) and a forbidden bribe (رشوة), and the conditions under which a judge may accept from relatives and customary givers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a judge accepting a gift. Determine the hukm level based on the stimulus. If the gift is given because of the judge's position, it is considered a bribe and is haram.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: 'Gifts in the time of the Prophet were gifts, today they are bribery.' This indicates that gifts motivated by the judge's position change category.
This is a Qawl Sahabi, considered zanni in both transmission and meaning but sufficient for the Hanafi ruling.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The statement distinguishes between an era when gifts were innocent (pre-appointment) and the current era where gifts are given due to position. The legal implication is that any gift given because of the judge's office is a bribe and thus haram.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is intra-Hanafi disagreement on acceptance from non-mahram without a prior custom. Some require a custom, others allow if the giver was poor and then became wealthy (Fakhr al-Islam's view).
The relied-upon view is the stricter one: acceptance only from mahram or those with an established custom.
5
Apply the ruling to other officials
The passage extends the ruling to other officials (imam, mufti) but with a difference: the mufti and imam may accept gifts because they are given for knowledge, not for judgment, unless the imam is the ruler.
The judge is uniquely restricted due to his role in litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء
الصلاة والسلام هلا جلس في بيت أبيه أو بيت أمه فينظر أيهدى إليه أم لا؟ قال عمر بن عبد العزيز رضي الله عنه : كانت الهدية على عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هدية واليوم رشوة. فتعليله دليل على تحريم الهدية التي سببها الولاية ويجب ردها على صاحبها فإن تعذر ردها على مالكها وضعها في بيت المال كاللقطة كما في فتح القدير فإن كان المهدي يتأذى بالرد يقبلها ويعطيه مثل قيمتها؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفي المضمرات : إذا دخلت الهدية له من الباب خرجت الأمانة . من الكوة. وقدمنا عن الأقطع الفرق بين الهدية والرشوة أن الرشوة ما كان معها شرط الإعانة بخلاف الهدية. وفي خزانة المفتين : مال يعطيه ولا يكون معها شرط، والرشوة مال يعطيه بشرط أن يعينه. وذكر الهدية في الكتاب ليس احترازياً إذ يحرم عليه الاستقراض والاستعارة ممن يحرم عليه قبول هديته كما في الخانية. وإنما يقبل هدية القريب لما فيها من صلة الرحم وردها قطعية وهي حرام وأطلقه وهو مقيد بالمحرم فخرج ابن العم مثلاً، ومقيد بأن لا تكون له خصومة وإنما يقبل ممن له عادة للعلم بأنها ليست للقضاء وله شرطان: أن لا يكون له خصومة، وأن لا يزيد على العادة فيرد الكل في الأول وما زاد عليها في الثاني. وقيده فخر الإسلام بأن لا يكون مال المهدي قد زاد فبقدر ما زاد ماله لا بأس بقبوله. وظاهر العطف في كلام المصنف يقتضي أنه يقبل من القريب وإن لم تكن له عادة بالإهداء. وفي ما كلام بعضهم يقتضي أنه كالأجنبي لا بد أن يكون له عادة وإلا فلا يقبلها منه إلا أن يكون لفقره ثم أيسر لأن الظاهر أن المانع ما كان إلا الفقر على وزان ما قاله فخر الإسلام في الزيادة والحاصل أن من له خصومة لا يقبلها مطلقاً، ومن لا خصومة له فإن كان له عادة قبل القضاء قبل المعتاد وإلا فلا. وفي تهذيب القلانسي : ولا يقبل هدية إلا من ذي رحم محرم أو من والي تولى الأمر منه أو وال مقدم الولاية على القضاء .اهـ. فعلى هذا له أن يقبلها السلطان من ومن حاكم بلده المسمى الآن بالباشاه واقتصر في التتارخانية على من وفي فتح القدير : وكل من عمل للمسلمين عملاً حكمه في الهدية حكم القاضي اهـ. فظاهره أنه يحرم قبولها على الوالي والمفتي وليس كما قال فقد قال في الخانية : ويجوز للإمام والمفتي قبول الهدية وإجابة الدعوة الخاصة لأن ذلك من حقوق المسلم على المسلم وإنما يمنع عنه القاضي .اهـ. إلا أن يراد بالإمام إمام الجامع. وفي التتارخانية من خصوصياته عليه السلام أن هداياه له وفيها ضم الواعظ إلى المفتي معللاً بأنه إنما يهدي إلى العالم لعلمه بخلاف القاضي. وأشار المصنف إلى أن القاضي لا يبيع ولا يشتري في مجلس القضاء وغيره وهو الصحيح لأن الناس يساهلونه لأجل القضاء؛ كذا في الخانية. هذا إذا كان يكفي المؤنة من بيت المال أو يعامل من يحابيه وإلا لا يكره ولو باع مال المديون أو الميت لا يكره؛ كذا في البزازية. وفي فتح القدير : ويجب أن يكون هدية المستقرض للمقرض كالهدية للقاضي إن كان المستقرض له عادة قبل استقراضه فأهدى إلى المقرض فللمقرض أن يقبل منه قدر ما كان يهديه بلا زيادة .اهـ. وهو سهو والمنقول كما قدمناه آخر الحوالة أنه يحل حيث لم يكن مشروطاً مطلقاً .
Chapter of Judgeship. ... (omitted for brevity) ... It is an error, and the transmitted ruling as we have mentioned at the end of Chapter of Hawala is that it is permissible when not conditional absolutely.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rules regarding gifts for judges. It begins by quoting 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: 'Gifts in the time of the Prophet were gifts, today they are bribery.' This shows that any gift motivated by the judge's position is forbidden. The gift must be returned to the giver; if impossible, it is placed in the public treasury. If the giver would be offended by refusal, the judge may accept and compensate its value. The key distinction between a gift (هدية) and a bribe (رشوة) is that a bribe comes with a condition of assistance. The judge may accept gifts only from a close relative (mahram) or from someone with a prior custom of giving, provided there is no litigation and the gift does not exceed the customary amount. There are additional conditions from later scholars: the giver's wealth should not have increased significantly. The same ruling applies to other officials like the ruler, governor, and mufti, though some allow gifts for them. The judge is also prohibited from buying and selling in the court due to suspicion, unless he has sufficient income from the treasury or deals with those who do not seek favor.

Key Terms
الهدية (al-hadiyya): A gift given without any condition or expectation of return.
الرشوة (al-rashwa): A bribe, i.e., a gift given with the condition of assistance or favor.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge entrusted with resolving disputes; subject to special restrictions on accepting gifts and invitations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic that turns a gift into a bribe for a judge?
A) The value of the gift exceeds a certain amount.
B) The gift is given with a condition of assistance or favor.
C) The gift is given during court proceedings.
D) The gift is from a non-relative.

Key Takeaway
A judge must refuse any gift given because of his position; only gifts from close relatives or customary givers free of litigation are permissible.

Accepting Special and Public Invitations: The Judge's Ethics
Intra-Hanafi dispute on judge's invitations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between special (khassa) and public ('amma) invitations for a judge, state the Hanafi ruling for each, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding special invitations.

Core Idea
A judge must decline a special invitation made because of his position, but may attend public feasts if the host has no pending litigation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a crucial aspect of judicial ethics: when may a qadi accept an invitation? The ruling hinges on the nature of the invitation—special or general—and the host's intent. This lesson unpacks the definitions, the Hanafi positions, and the practical guidelines for a judge's conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of invitations
Read the passage to distinguish between 'special' (khassa) and 'public' ('amma) invitations. Note that the ruling differs for each.
The distinction is based on the host's intention and custom.
2
State the ruling for special invitations and the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Identify the ruling: the judge must decline special invitations. Then note the dispute: Sahibayn say decline; Muhammad al-Shaybani says accept. The majority view is to decline.
The author of al-Kafi favored Muhammad's view, but the text recommends the stricter view.
3
Understand the definition of 'special' vs 'general' as per al-Hidayah
Learn the preferred definition: a special invitation is one the host would not have made if the judge would not attend; a general invitation is made regardless. Also note other opinions (by number, by occasion).
Al-Hidayah's definition is the most practical and is adopted by many later scholars.
4
Apply the rule to custom-based invitations
Analyze the exception: if a person habitually invites the judge (e.g., monthly dinner), the judge may accept. But if the habit changes after his appointment, he must decline.
This prevents abuse and maintains the judge's impartiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودعوة خاصة ) أي يردها فلا يحضرها لأنها جعلت لأجله. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الداعي لها القريب. وذكر الطحاوي أن هذا قولهما. وقال محمد: يجيبها. وذكر الخصاف أنه يجيبها بلا خلاف واختاره المؤلف في الكافي. وإنما ترك التقييد به في المختصر اعتماداً على ما استثناه في الهدية فالأحسن أن يقال : ولا يقبل هدية ودعوى خاصة إلا من محرم أو ممن له عادة فإن للقاضي أن يجيب الدعوة الخاصة من أجنبي له عادة باتخاذها كالهدية، فلو كان من عادته الدعوة له كل شهر مرة فدعاه كل أسبوع بعد القضاء لا يجيبه . ، ولو اتخذ له طعاماً أكثر من الأول لا يجيبه إلا أن يكون ماله قد زاد ؛ كذا في التتارخانية. قيد بالخاصة احترازا عن العامة فإن له أن يحضرها بشرط أن لا يكون لصاحبها خصومة. واختلف في الخاصة والعامة فقيل ما دون العشرة خاصة والعشرة وما فوقها عامة. واختار في الهداية أن الخاصة هي ما لو علم صاحبها أن القاضي لا يحضرها لا يتخذها، والعامة هي التي يتخذها وإن لم يحضرها. وحكي عن أبي علي النسفي أن العامة دعوة العرس والختان وما سواهما خاصة. وفي فتح القدير عندي أنه حسن لأن الغالب أن العامة هاتان وربما مضى عمر ولم نعرف من اصطنع طعاماً عاماً ابتداء لعامة الناس بل ليس إلا هاتين الخصلتين أو بخصوص من الناس أو لكونه أضبط فإن معرفة كون الرجل لو لم يحضر القاضي لم يصنع أو يصنع غير محقق فإنه أمر مبطن وإن كان عليه لوائح ليس كضبط هذا وتكفي عادة الناس في وعادة الناس هي ما ذكر النسفي اهـ وعندي أنه ليس بحسن لأن العامة عرفاً لا تنحصر في هاتين لأن العقيقة كذلك، وكذا طعام القدوم من سفر الحج، وفي زماننا يصنع طعام عام في العيدين فالمعتمد ما في الهداية. وفي السراج الوهاج أنه أصح ما قيل في تفسيرها اهـ. واختاره شمس الأئمة السرخسي كما في المعراج. وفي الخلاصة: وهو الصحيح وجزم به قاضيخان في فتاواه بقوله : وإنما يعرف الخاص من العام إلى آخره. ولم ذلك صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم قبولها وإلا انتفت الخصوصية تأمل، ثم رأيته في النهر بحث كذلك وهذا
His saying: (and a special invitation) i.e., he should refuse it and not attend because it is made for his sake. He gave it absolutely, so it includes even if the inviter is a relative. Al-Tahawi mentions that this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Muhammad said: he may accept it. Al-Khassaf mentions that he may accept it without dispute, and the author in al-Kafi chose it. He only left the restriction in al-Mukhtar relying on what he excepted in the gift, so it is better to say: he accepts neither gift nor special invitation except from a mahram or someone with a habit; for the judge may accept a special invitation from a stranger who has a habit of making such invitations, like the gift. ...
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a judge may accept special (خاصة) and general (عامة) invitations. The key ruling: the judge must decline special invitations that are made specifically because of his position. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn), the judge must decline; Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani alone holds that he may accept. The author of al-Kafi prefers the latter, but the more cautious view is that acceptance is only from a mahram or from someone with a prior custom of inviting. General invitations, such as weddings or circumcision feasts, may be attended if the host has no litigation. The passage also discusses definitions: some consider fewer than ten guests as special; al-Hidayah defines special as that which would not be made if the judge would not attend; general is that which is made regardless. The correct view is that of al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الدعوة الخاصة (al-da'wa al-khassa): A private invitation that would not be made if the judge were not a judge; it is forbidden for him to attend if it is for his position.
الدعوة العامة (al-da'wa al-'amma): A public invitation like weddings or circumcision feasts open to many; judge may attend if host has no litigation.
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood, marriage, or fosterage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining criterion for a special invitation (خاصة) as preferred in al-Hidayah?
A) The number of guests is less than ten.
B) The invitation is for a wedding or circumcision.
C) The host would not have made the invitation if he knew the judge would not attend.
D) The judge has a prior relationship with the host.

Key Takeaway
A judge must decline special invitations motivated by his position, but may accept public feasts and customary invitations from mahram or habitual inviters, provided no litigation and no change in habit after appointment.

Rulings on Gifts and Transactions for the Judge
Ruling statement on judges' trade and gifts with exceptions and a correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a judge's buying and selling, identify the exceptions, and evaluate the dispute over a borrower's gift to a lender.

Core Idea
A judge must avoid gifts and trade to preserve impartiality, but necessity can lift the prohibition.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on gifts and commercial transactions involving a judge. While a mufti may accept gifts for his knowledge, a judge must be cautious to avoid bias. The following passage clarifies the ruling and its exceptions, including a critical note on a related case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Locate the statement: the judge should not buy or sell in or out of court. Determine the hukm: makruh tanzih (disliked).
This is the relied-upon view.
2
Distinguish between mufti and qadi
Note why a mufti can accept gifts (for his knowledge) while a qadi cannot (due to authority).
The key difference is the potential for influence.
3
Identify the exceptions
Find when the prohibition is lifted: when the judge's expenses are covered by the treasury, or when dealing with someone who would not show favoritism. Also, selling the property of a debtor or deceased is not disliked.
These exceptions show necessity overrides the default ruling.
4
Analyze the borrower-lender gift issue
Review the passage from Fath al-Qadir equating a borrower's gift to a lender with a judge's gift. Note the commentator's correction: it is permissible if not conditioned.
This is a methodological correction; the earlier view is deemed an oversight.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
Summarize the operative rulings: a judge should avoid trade, but exceptions exist; the borrower-lender gift is allowed if not conditional.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء والسلام أن هداياه له وفيها ضم الواعظ إلى المفتي معللاً بأنه إنما يهدي إلى العالم لعلمه بخلاف القاضي. وأشار المصنف إلى أن القاضي لا يبيع ولا يشتري في مجلس القضاء وغيره وهو الصحيح لأن الناس يساهلونه لأجل القضاء؛ كذا في الخانية. هذا إذا كان يكفي المؤنة من بيت المال أو يعامل من يحابيه وإلا لا يكره ولو باع مال المديون أو الميت لا يكره؛ كذا في البزازية. وفي فتح القدير : ويجب أن يكون هدية المستقرض للمقرض كالهدية للقاضي إن كان المستقرض له عادة قبل استقراضه فأهدى إلى المقرض فللمقرض أن يقبل منه قدر ما كان يهديه بلا زيادة .اهـ. وهو سهو والمنقول كما قدمناه آخر الحوالة أنه يحل حيث لم يكن مشروطاً مطلقاً.
The Book of Judiciary: And the greeting that his gifts... In it, the preacher is included with the mufti, explaining that he only gives gifts to the scholar for his knowledge, unlike the judge. The author indicated that the judge should not buy or sell in the court session or elsewhere, and this is the correct view because people indulge him on account of his judgeship; thus in al-Khaniyyah. This applies if his living expenses are covered from the treasury or if he deals with those who would show him favoritism; otherwise, it is not disliked. If he sells the property of a debtor or a deceased, it is not disliked; thus in al-Bazzaziyyah. In Fath al-Qadir: The gift from a borrower to a lender must be like the gift to a judge if the borrower had a habit before borrowing and then gave a gift to the lender—the lender may accept the same amount he used to give without increase. End. This is an oversight; the transmitted view is as we presented at the end of al-Hawalah: it is permissible if not absolutely conditional.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding gifts to judges. It begins by distinguishing between a mufti and a judge: people give gifts to a mufti for his knowledge, which is permissible, but for a judge it is problematic due to potential bias. The author then states that a judge should not engage in buying or selling in or out of court because people might show leniency towards him due to his position. This ruling is from al-Khaniyyah. However, if the judge's needs are met by the treasury or if he deals with someone who would not show favoritism, it is not disliked. Selling the property of a debtor or deceased is exempt from this restriction. The passage then addresses a case from Fath al-Qadir regarding a borrower's gift to a lender, equating it to the judge's case: if the borrower had a prior habit of giving, the lender may accept the same amount without increase. The author notes this is an oversight; the correct transmitted view is that it is permissible if not conditioned absolutely.

Key Terms
قاضي (qadi): The judge is a person appointed to adjudicate disputes; receiving gifts from litigants is prohibited due to potential bias.
هدية (hadiyyah): A gift given to the judge; it is prohibited if given on account of his position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When he sells to a litigant.
C) When he sells the property of a debtor or a deceased person.
D) When he sells to a family member.

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid gifts and trade to maintain impartiality, but exceptions exist for necessity and selling debtor's property.
