Rulings on Special Invitations for the Judge
Intra-Hanafi dispute on judge's acceptance of special invitations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between special and general invitations for a judge and state the relied-upon Hanafi position on acceptance.

Core Idea
A judge must reject a special invitation made on account of his position, but may accept general invitations if no litigation exists.

The judge's impartiality is paramount. Islamic law prohibits a judge from accepting invitations that are made specifically because of his position, as they may influence his judgment. However, not all invitations are prohibited; general feasts open to the public are permissible under conditions. The Hanafi school has internal differences on the definition of 'special' vs 'general' and whether a judge may ever accept a special invitation. This lesson unpacks these rulings and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling on special invitations
The matn states that a special invitation (da'wah khassah) is to be rejected—the judge should not attend because it is made on his account. This applies even if the inviter is a relative.
2
Note the ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Khassaf hold that the judge may accept a special invitation. Al-Tahawi reports this is only the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, while Muhammad himself said to accept. The author of al-Kafi chose acceptance.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
3
Understand the definitions of special vs general
Three definitions are given: numerical (less than ten is special), intention-based (special if host would not prepare it without the judge), and occasion-based (only wedding and circumcision are general). The relied-upon view is the intention-based definition from al-Hidayah.
4
Evaluate the tarjih and exceptions
The majority of later Hanafi authorities (al-Sarakhsi, Qadikhan) adopt the intention-based definition. Exceptions: the judge may accept from a mahram or from someone with a custom of inviting (e.g., monthly dinner).
5
Apply the ruling with conditions for general invitations
General invitations may be accepted on condition that the host has no litigation with the judge. The judge should assess the intent behind the invitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودعوة خاصة أي يردها فلا يحضرها لأنها جعلت لأجله. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الداعي لها القريب. وذكر الطحاوي أن هذا قولهما. وقال محمد: يجيبها. وذكر الخصاف أنه يجيبها بلا خلاف واختاره المؤلف في الكافي. وإنما ترك التقييد به في المختصر اعتماداً على ما استثناه في الهدية فالأحسن أن يقال : ولا يقبل هدية ودعوى خاصة إلا من محرم أو ممن له عادة فإن للقاضي أن يجيب الدعوة الخاصة من أجنبي له عادة باتخاذها كالهدية، فلو كان من عادته الدعوة له كل شهر مرة فدعاه كل أسبوع بعد القضاء لا يجيبه . ، ولو اتخذ له طعاماً أكثر من الأول لا يجيبه إلا أن يكون ماله قد زاد ؛ كذا في التتارخانية. قيد بالخاصة احترازا عن العامة فإن له أن يحضرها بشرط أن لا يكون لصاحبها خصومة. واختلف في الخاصة والعامة فقيل ما دون العشرة خاصة والعشرة وما فوقها عامة. واختار في الهداية أن الخاصة هي ما لو علم صاحبها أن القاضي لا يحضرها لا يتخذها، والعامة هي التي يتخذها وإن لم يحضرها. وحكي عن أبي علي النسفي أن العامة دعوة العرس والختان وما سواهما خاصة. وفي فتح القدير عندي أنه حسن لأن الغالب أن العامة هاتان وربما مضى عمر ولم نعرف من اصطنع طعاماً عاماً ابتداء لعامة الناس بل ليس إلا هاتين الخصلتين أو بخصوص من الناس أو لكونه أضبط فإن معرفة كون الرجل لو لم يحضر القاضي لم يصنع أو يصنع غير محقق فإنه أمر مبطن وإن كان عليه لوائح ليس كضبط هذا وتكفي عادة الناس في وعادة الناس هي ما ذكر النسفي اهـ وعندي أنه ليس بحسن لأن العامة عرفاً لا تنحصر في هاتين لأن العقيقة كذلك، وكذا طعام القدوم من سفر الحج، وفي زماننا يصنع طعام عام في العيدين فالمعتمد ما في الهداية. وفي السراج الوهاج أنه أصح ما قيل في تفسيرها اهـ. واختاره شمس الأئمة السرخسي كما في المعراج. وفي الخلاصة: وهو الصحيح وجزم به قاضيخان في فتاواه بقوله : وإنما يعرف الخاص من العام إلى آخره. ولم ذلك صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم قبولها وإلا انتفت الخصوصية تأمل، ثم رأيته في النهر بحث كذلك وهذا
His saying: (And a special invitation meaning he should reject it and not attend because it is made on his account. He made it absolute, so it includes when the inviter is a relative. Al-Tahawi mentioned that this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: He should accept it. And al-Khassaf mentioned that he accepts it without dispute, and the author chose it in al-Kafi. He only left the qualification in al-Mukhtasar relying on what he excepted in the gift, so it is better to say: He should not accept a gift or a special invitation except from a mahram or from one who has a custom; then the judge may accept the special invitation from a stranger who has a custom of making it, like the gift. If his custom was to invite him once a month, and then he invites him every week after the judgment, he should not go. And if he makes more food than before, he should not go unless his wealth increased; thus in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. He qualified it with 'special' to exclude general invitations, for he may attend them on condition that the host has no litigation. There is dispute over special and general: some say less than ten is special, ten or more is general. Al-Hidayah chose that special is what the host would not prepare if he knew the judge would not attend, and general is what he would prepare even if the judge did not attend. It is narrated from Abu Ali al-Nasafi that general is the wedding and circumcision feasts, and other than those are special. In Fath al-Qadir: I think this is good because generally general feasts are these two, and perhaps one goes through life not knowing of anyone making a general feast from the start for all people; there are only these two occasions or specific people, or because it is more precise, for knowing whether a man would not make it if the judge did not attend is not confirmed as it is a hidden matter, though there may be indications, it is not like this precision, and custom suffices, and custom is what al-Nasafi mentioned. End. And I think it is not good because generally by custom it is not limited to these two, because the 'aqiqah is also such, likewise the feast for returning from Hajj, and in our time a general feast is made on the two 'Eids. So the relied-upon view is what is in al-Hidayah. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj it is said to be the most correct interpretation. End. And Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi chose it as in al-Mi'raj. In al-Khulasa: It is the correct view, and Qadikhan affirmed it in his Fatawa saying: Indeed the special is known from the general... and that the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted it, otherwise specificity would be negated. Ponder. Then I saw in al-Nahr a discussion similarly and this.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the judge's acceptance of special invitations. It begins by explaining the matn phrase 'special invitation': the judge should reject it and not attend because it is made for his sake. The ruling includes even if the inviter is a relative. There is a known ikhtilaf: al-Tahawi reports that this is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, while Muhammad himself (Imam Muhammad) said the judge should accept. Al-Khassaf reported that he accepts without dispute, and the author of al-Kafi chose that. The text suggests a better formulation: the judge should not accept a gift or a special invitation except from a mahram or from someone with a custom of inviting. Then it details conditions: if the inviter had a custom of inviting once a month, then invites every week after the judgment, the judge should not go. If the food quantity increases, he should not go unless the inviter's wealth increased. The definition of 'special' vs 'general' is contested. Three definitions are given: (1) numerical: less than ten is special; (2) intention-based: special is what would not be prepared if the judge were not attending; general is prepared regardless; (3) occasion-based (from al-Nasafi): general is only wedding and circumcision; others are special. The author of Fath al-Qadir initially favors al-Nasafi's view as more precise, but then the commentator ('I') criticizes it, arguing that custom also includes 'aqiqah, return from Hajj, and in his time 'Eid feasts. So the relied-upon view is the intention-based definition from al-Hidayah, also chosen by al-Sarakhsi and Qadikhan. The passage ends with a note that if the judge accepts, it negates the specificity, and references al-Nahr.

Key Terms
دعوة خاصة (da'wah khassah): A private invitation made specifically on account of the judge's position; rejecting it is required.
دعوة عامة (da'wah 'ammah): A public invitation not directed at the judge personally; the judge may attend if the host has no litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, how is a 'special invitation' (دعوة خاصة) defined?
A) Any invitation with fewer than ten guests.
B) An invitation that the host would not have made if the judge were not coming.
C) An invitation for a wedding or circumcision.
D) Any invitation from a relative.

Key Takeaway
A judge must reject a special invitation made on account of his position; 'special' is defined by the host's intent, not by numbers or occasions, with exceptions for mahram and custom.

The Judge's Duties: Funerals, Sick Visits, and Equality
Explains the judge's obligations to attend funerals, visit the sick, and maintain equality between litigants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's obligations to attend funerals and visit the sick, identify the conditions for these visits, and apply the rule of equality between litigants to diverse scenarios including rulers and dhimmis.

Core Idea
The judge must treat all litigants equally externally; internal partiality is not punishable.

A judge's duties extend beyond the courtroom. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq outlines three key obligations: attending funerals, visiting the sick, and ensuring perfect external equality between litigants. These are rooted in the Islamic rights of brotherhood and the need to maintain justice visibly. We will unpack the hadith evidence and the practical application of these rules, including stories from early Hanafi judges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three duties
Read the beginning of the passage: the judge must attend funerals (shuhud al-janazah), visit the sick (iyadat al-marid), and make litigants equal in sitting (taswiyah). Note that equality is elaborated extensively.
The duty of equality is emphasized with details on sitting, looking, gesturing, and voice.
2
Examine the evidence for funerals and sick visits
The text cites the hadith of six rights. The judge's duty is not dropped due to his position, but he should not prolong his stay and should only visit if the person has no litigation against him.
Conditions: no pending case and no prolonged absence from court.
3
Analyze the equality requirement
Equality in seating is wajib based on the hadith 'if one of you is afflicted with judgment...' It includes all litigants regardless of status, even the sultan or a dhimmi. The text provides the famous story of Abu Yusuf with Harun al-Rashid.
External equality is required; internal feelings are mustahabb but not enforceable.
4
Understand the scope and exceptions
Equality covers sitting, looking, gesturing, and tone. The judge may not prompt or host one litigant. However, if the sick person has a pending case, the judge should not visit to avoid suspicion. Also, equality does not require the judge to equalize in his heart.
Practical limits: the judge may have to step down from his seat to seat a commoner if a sultan is also present (al-Nawazil example).
5
Learn about litigants' posture and additional admonitions
The passage concludes with etiquette: litigants should crouch, not sit cross-legged or on their haunches. The judge may enforce respectful behavior. A brief critique of al-Nasafi's definition is noted.
Focus on the main rulings; the critique is secondary.

وغيره ولذا قال في النوازل والفتاوي الكبرى : خاصم السلطان رجل فجلس السلطان مع القاضي في مجلسه ينبغي للقاضي أن يقوم من مقامه ويجلس خصم السلطان فيه ويقعد هو على الأرض ثم يقضي بينهما اهـ. وهذا دليل على أن أفضل فقد حكى في الولوالجية أن أبا يوسف وقت موته قال: اللهم إنك تعلم أني لم أمل إلى أحد الخصمين حتى بالقلب إلا في خصومة نصراني مع الرشيد لم أسو بينهما وقضيت على الرشيد ثم بكى. ومما حكي عن أبي يوسف أن وارد. (١) رواه البخاري في كتاب الأدب باب ١٢٥ مسلم في كتاب الزهد حديث ٥٤. أبو داود في الأدب باب ١، ٤ . النسائي في كتاب الجنائز باب .١ أحمد في مسنده (۱/ ۸۹) (٢/ ٦٨، ٣٢١).
And he visits the sick and makes them (the litigants) equal in sitting, and he should avoid whispering with one, gesturing, prompting his argument, and his hospitality. (Then a critique of al-Nasafi's definition: it is not a standard, let alone being more precise; that he only makes it for the judge is not hidden, and some is known by explicit statement, some by circumstances like explicit.) His saying: (And he attends the funeral and visits the sick) because this is a right of a Muslim upon a Muslim. In the hadith: 'A Muslim has six rights upon another Muslim: when he invites him, he should respond; when he is sick, he should visit him; when he dies, he should attend his funeral; when he meets him, he should greet him; when he seeks his advice, he should advise him; and when he sneezes, he should say yarhamukallah.' (1) Thus in al-Nihayah, and it is not dropped due to judgeship, but he should not prolong his stay in that place, and he visits on condition that the person has no litigation; otherwise no. His saying: (And make them equal in sitting) meaning the judge must make the two litigants equal in sitting for the hadith: 'If one of you is afflicted with judgment, let him make them equal in the gathering, in looking and gesturing, and let him not raise his voice to one litigant over the other.' Reported by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and similarly by al-Daraqutni. And because inequality breaks the heart of the other. So he sits them in front of him and does not seat one on his right and the other on his left because the right has virtue. He made absolute equality, so it includes the noble and the lowly, father and son, young and old, free and slave, sultan and others. Hence it is said in al-Nawazil and al-Fatawa al-Kubra: A sultan disputed with a man, and the sultan sat with the judge in his seat. The judge should get up from his place, seat the sultan's opponent in it, and sit on the ground, then judge between them. End. This is proof that the judge judges over the sultan who appointed him, and the proof is the story of Shuraih with 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with them). It includes Muslim and dhimmi, so he makes them equal as in Fatawa Qari' al-Hidayah. He qualified it with sitting because he is not obliged to equalize in his heart, though it is better. It is narrated in al-Walwaljiyyah that Abu Yusuf at the time of his death said: O Allah, You know that I did not incline to either litigant even in my heart except in a case of a Christian with al-Rashid; I did not make them equal and I decided against al-Rashid, then he wept. And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that a servant of the caliph's chief servants came with his opponent, and he raised his hand against him, so Abu Yusuf ordered equality but he did not comply. Abu Yusuf said: 'By the back! O boy, bring me 'Umar and the slave trader to sell this servant and send his price to the Commander of the Faithful.' So he became equal and the case was resolved. The servant went to the caliph and told him what happened, weeping profusely. The caliph said: 'If he sold you, I would have ratified the sale and not returned you to my ownership. May Allah have mercy on him.' And the two litigants should crouch before him and not sit cross-legged, squat, or sit on their haunches; if they do, the judge should prevent them out of respect, emphasizing that the reference to 'Imam' in al-Khaniyyah means the Imam of the mosque. His saying: (And I think it is not good, etc.) said in al-Nahr: And you are aware that this counts as... (the hadith sources follow).
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings on the judge's conduct. It first addresses visiting the sick and attending funerals, stating that these are rights of a Muslim upon a Muslim based on a hadith. The judge should not neglect these duties, but he must not prolong his stay and should only visit if the person has no litigation against him. The next ruling is on equality between litigants: the judge must treat them equally in sitting, looking, and gesturing, based on a hadith and rational reasoning. Equality is required regardless of social status, including between a sultan and a commoner, father and son, Muslim and dhimmi. The text illustrates with a story of Abu Yusuf judging against Caliph al-Rashid, showing that even the ruler is not above the law. The judge should physically sit litigants in front of him, not on either side, to avoid giving preference. Internal equality (in heart) is not obligatory but recommended. The passage then discusses the proper posture for litigants: they should not sit cross-legged etc., and the judge may enforce respectful behavior. Finally, it returns to the earlier debate on the definition of general/special invitation with a note from al-Nahr criticizing the author's earlier view that general feasts are only two types. The hadith sources for the six rights are cited.

Key Terms
التسوية بين الخصمين (al-taswiyah bayna al-khasmayn): The judge's duty to treat both litigants equally in seating, looking, and gesture; any partiality is prohibited.
شهود الجنازة (shuhud al-janazah): Attending the funeral of a Muslim; a right that the judge must fulfill unless there is a conflict of interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what must a judge do if a sultan and a commoner appear as litigants?
A) Seat the sultan in his own seat and the commoner on the floor.
B) Treat them equally, even if it means the judge sits on the floor.
C) Give the sultan extra respect due to his position.
D) Hear the sultan's case privately.

Key Takeaway
The judge must treat all litigants equally in external actions (sitting, looking, gesturing, voice); internal feelings are not legally required, but external equality is wajib.

Judge's Duty to Attend Funerals and Visit the Sick
Ruling on judge's social duties with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a judge attending funerals and visiting the sick, and identify the condition that restricts this duty.

Core Idea
A judge's personal Islamic duties remain, but potential conflict limits his visits.

This lesson covers a judge's personal obligations as a Muslim. The hadith of six rights establishes that attending funerals and visiting the sick are communal duties, but the judge's role imposes conditions to avoid partiality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
Note the acts: attending the funeral (shuhud al-janazah) and visiting the sick (iyadat al-marid). The text states they are rights of a Muslim over a Muslim — not dropped due to being a judge.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the well-known hadith enumerating six rights (al-salāmu, al-iyāda, al-janāzah, etc.).
Hadith is from al-Nihayah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The listing indicates a recommended duty (sunnah muakkadah) for every Muslim, including the judge. The judge is not excused because his position does not cancel personal obligations.
Hanafi usul: commands in such contexts indicate recommendation unless evidence shows obligation.
4
Note the condition
The judge must not prolong his stay, and he should only visit if there is no litigation between him and the sick/deceased. Otherwise, he refrains to avoid bias.
Condition: 'bi-sharṭi an lā khuṣūmata lahu' — no lawsuit.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والدليل عليه قصة شريح مع علي رضي الله عنه . وشمل المسلم والذمي فيسوي بينهما كما في فتاوى قارئ الهداية. وقيد بالجلوس لأنه لا يجب عليه التسوية بينهما بالقلب وإن كان أفضل فقد حكى في الولوالجية أن أبا يوسف وقت موته قال: اللهم إنك تعلم أني لم أمل إلى أحد الخصمين حتى بالقلب إلا في خصومة نصراني مع الرشيد لم أسو بينهما وقضيت على الرشيد ثم بكى. ومما حكي عن أبي يوسف أن خادماً من أكبر خدام الخليفة جاء مع خصمه للدعوى فترافع على خصمه فأمره أبو يوسف بالمساواة فلم يمتثل فقال القفا يا غلام ائتني بعمر والنخاس يبيع هذا الخادم وأرسل ثمنه إلى أمير المؤمنين فاستوى وانقضت الدعوى فذهب الخادم إلى الخليفة وقص عليه ما جرى وبكى بكاء شديداً فقال له : لو باعك لأجزت بيعه ولم أردك إلى ملكي رحمه الله تعالى. وينبغي للخصمين أن يحثوا بين يديه ولا يتربعان ولا يقعيان ولا يجتبيان ولو فعلا ذلك منعهما القاضي تعظيماً يؤكد حمل الأمام في كلام الخانية على إمام الجامع. قوله : ( وعندي إنه ليس بحسن الخ) قال في النهر : وأنت خبير بأن هذا يعد أن أدعى أن الغالب كون الدعوة العامة هاتين غير وارد. والمزاح وتلقين الشاهد. الخلاف للحكم كما يجلس المتعلم بين يدي المعلم تعظيماً .له ويكون بعدهما عنه قدر ذراعين أو نحو ذلك من غير أن يرفعا أصواتهما. وتقف أعوان القاضي بين يديه فيكون أهيب. وقدمنا بين الشيخين في ابتداء القاضي لهما بالسؤال وفي فتح القدير هنا، والأصح عندنا أنه يستنطقه ابتداء للعلم بالمقصود ولا يتعجل على الخصوم ولا يخوفهم وينبغي أن يقوم بين يديه إذا جلس للحكم رجل يمنع الناس عن التقدم إليه معه سوط يقال له الجلواز وصاحب المجلس يقيم الخصوم بين يديه على البعد والشهود بقرب من القاضي .
His saying: (He attends the funeral and visits the sick) because this is a right of a Muslim over a Muslim. In the hadith: 'For a Muslim over a Muslim are six rights: when he invites him, he responds; when he is sick, he visits him; when he dies, he attends him; when he meets him, he greets him; when he seeks his advice, he advises him; when he sneezes, he says yarhamukallah.' Thus in al-Nihayah. It is not dropped due to being a judge, but he should not prolong his stay in that place. He only visits on condition that there is no litigation against him; otherwise no. His saying: (And let them sit equally between them) meaning it is obligatory upon the judge to equalize between the two litigants in seating, due to the hadith: 'When one of you is afflicted with judging, then let them equalize between them in the gathering, glance, and gesture, and let him not raise his voice against one litigant over the other.' Narrated by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and similarly by al-Daraqutni. And because inequality causes heartbreak for the other. So he seats them both before him, and does not seat one on his right and the other on his left because the right side has virtue. He made it absolute in equality, including the noble and the lowly, father and son, small and large, free and slave, sultan and others. Hence it is said in al-Nawazil and al-Fatawa al-Kubra: A sultan sued a man, and the sultan sat with the judge in his seat. The judge should stand from his place, seat the sultan's opponent in it, sit on the ground, and then judge between them. This is evidence that the judge judges over the sultan who appointed him, and the evidence is the story of Shuraih with Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). It includes Muslim and dhimmi; he equalizes between them as in Fatawa Qari al-Hidayah. It is restricted to seating because equality in heart is not obligatory upon him, though it is better. It is narrated in al-Walwaljiyyah that Abu Yusuf at the time of his death said: 'O Allah, You know that I did not incline towards either litigant, even in heart, except in a case of a Christian with al-Rashid; I did not equalize between them and ruled against al-Rashid.' Then he wept. Among what is narrated from Abu Yusuf is that a servant from the greatest servants of the caliph came with his opponent for a claim, and he sued his opponent. Abu Yusuf ordered him to equalize but he did not comply. So he said: 'O boy, bring me Umar and the slave dealer to sell this servant, and send his price to the Commander of the Faithful.' Then he equalized and the case concluded. The servant went to the caliph and told him what happened, weeping intensely. The caliph said: 'If he had sold you, I would have ratified his sale and would not return you to my ownership. May Allah have mercy on him.' It is recommended that the two litigants sit on their knees before him, not cross-legged, not squatting, and not sitting on their buttocks with knees drawn. If they do that, the judge prevents them out of reverence. The statement 'imam' in the speech of al-Khaniyyah is interpreted as the imam of the mosque. His saying: (And according to me it is not good etc.) Al-Nahr said: You are aware that this is considered... the majority being that the general claim is not relevant. And joking and prompting the witness. The difference for the ruler is like the student sitting before the teacher out of reverence, and he should be about two cubits or so away from them without raising their voices. The judge's assistants stand before him, which is more awe-inspiring. We have presented between the two sheikhs regarding the judge's initial questioning. In Fath al-Qadir here, the most correct according to us is that he questions initially to learn the objective, and he should not be hasty with the litigants nor frighten them. It is recommended that a man stand before him when he sits for judgment to prevent people from coming forward, with a whip called al-jaluwaz, and the master of the assembly makes the litigants stand before him at a distance, while the witnesses are near the judge.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the judge's personal duties and courtroom conduct. The first ruling concerns attending funerals and visiting the sick. The commentator cites the well-known hadith of six rights of a Muslim over another, indicating these are communal obligations. However, the judge is not excused due to his position; he still must fulfill these rights, but he should not prolong his visit, and he should only visit a sick person if there is no litigation between them. The second major ruling is the obligation (wajib) of equality between litigants in seating. The commentator provides a hadith with a direct command to equalize in the gathering, glance, and gesture, and not to raise voice against one. The reason given is avoiding heartbreak of the other. The equality applies regardless of social status, religion, or relationship, including between a sultan and a commoner. A famous example is quoted: if a sultan sits with the judge, the judge should stand, seat the opponent in his place, and sit on the ground to demonstrate equality. This shows the judge can judge over the sultan. The equality is limited to external actions; internal feelings are not required, though it is better. The story of Abu Yusuf at his death shows how seriously he took external equality. Another story illustrates Abu Yusuf's firmness: he threatened to sell a powerful servant who refused to sit equally. Additional courtroom etiquette is discussed: litigants should sit on their knees, not cross-legged or squatting, out of respect. The text also mentions the role of assistants and the use of a whip (jaluwaz) to maintain order. The judge should initiate questioning and not hurry or frighten litigants.

Key Terms
تسوية (taswiyah): Making equal; the judge's duty to treat both litigants identically in outward manners like seating, glance, and speech.
مكسرة (mukassirah): Something that causes heartbreak or humiliation; inequality in treatment is a cause.
جلواز (jaluwaz): An officer in court with a whip to maintain order and prevent people from rushing towards the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition does the Hanafi school place on a judge visiting the sick?
A) He must visit all sick Muslims.
B) He should not visit if the person is a litigant in his court.
C) He must send a deputy.
D) He can only visit on weekends.

Key Takeaway
A judge fulfills his social duties as long as they do not conflict with his judicial impartiality.

Qada — Judiciary

Equality Between Litigants in Court: The Judge's Obligation
Ruling on equality between litigants with proof and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on equality between litigants, apply the hadith proof, and explain the scope including treatment of high and low parties.

Core Idea
Equality in seating and gaze is wajib; even the sultan must sit equally with his opponent.

This lesson explores one of the most fundamental duties of a judge: treating both litigants equally in external manners. The Hanafi school holds this as wajib based on a specific hadith, and the ruling applies to all persons regardless of status. Stories of Abu Yusuf illustrate the seriousness of this obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The judge must equalize between the two litigants in seating, glance, and gesture. The text says 'yajibu' (it is obligatory).
Hanafi: wajib, not fard, because the dalil is ahad.
2
Identify the dalil
The hadith: 'When one of you is afflicted with judging, then let them equalize between them in the gathering, glance, and gesture, and let him not raise his voice against one over the other.'
Narrated by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and al-Daraqutni.
3
Analyse the scope
Equality applies to all: noble and lowly, father and son, free and slave, sultan and commoner, Muslim and dhimmi. The text gives the example of a sultan sitting with the judge — the judge must stand and seat the opponent.
Includes non-Muslim litigants.
4
Note the limitation
Equality is required only in external actions (seating, glance, gesture, voice). Equality in the heart is not obligatory, though it is better.
Based on Abu Yusuf's deathbed statement.
5
Apply the stories
The stories of Abu Yusuf illustrate: (1) realizing he had not equalized even in heart once, and weeping; (2) threatening to sell a powerful servant who refused to sit equally, which resulted in compliance.
Shows the practical enforcement of equality.

What a Judge Must Avoid: Whispering, Gesturing, Prompting, Hospitality, and Jest
Rulings on prohibited behaviors for a judge in court

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five prohibited actions for a judge in court and explain why each is prohibited, citing the hadith of Ali (RA) for hospitality and the intra-Hanafi dispute on prompting.

Core Idea
A judge must avoid any action that creates suspicion of bias: whispering, gesturing, prompting, hosting one litigant, and jesting.

In Hanafi judiciary (qada), the integrity of the judge is paramount. The court must be a space of absolute impartiality. This lesson from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary identifies five specific behaviors a judge must avoid, each carrying the potential to undermine public trust in the verdict. We will examine each prohibition, the evidence, and the scholarly disagreement on prompting witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Prohibited Actions
List the five actions: whispering (musararah), gesturing (isharah), prompting evidence (talqin hujjah), hosting one litigant (diyafah), and jesting (mizah). Understand that they are all prohibited because they create suspicion (tuhma).
These are the exact terms from the matn.
2
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Illah)
The illah (effective cause) for the prohibition is the appearance of partiality. Even if the judge's heart is pure, these actions break the heart of the other litigant and erode trust in the judiciary.
The text says 'because it involves suspicion and breaking the heart of the other.'
3
Examine the Evidence for Hospitality
Analyze the mursal hadith of Ali (RA): a man who was hosted by Ali later said he wanted to sue, and Ali told him to leave, citing the Prophet's prohibition of hosting a litigant without his opponent. This is the primary dalil for the prohibition.
The hadith is zanni in transmission but used as evidence.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Prompting
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit prompting absolutely. Abu Yusuf allows it outside of suspicion as a concession, returning to this view after his judicial experience. The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view for judiciary matters.
The texts (Fath al-Qadir, al-Qunyah) prefer Abu Yusuf's position.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Practical Application
While the determination ('azimah) is with Sahibayn, the fatwa for judges today is to follow Abu Yusuf's leniency in prompting witnesses, but always avoiding any suspicion. Joking is prohibited in court but permissible elsewhere in moderation.
The passage also discusses appointing assistants to learn the claim, which is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وليتق) عن مسارة أحدهما وإشارته وتلقين حجته وضيافته) أي وليجتنب عن الأشياء لأن فيها تهمة ومكسرة لقلب الآخر والمسارة من ساره في أذنه وتساروا تناجوا؛ كذا في القاموس. والمعنى أنه يجتنب الكلام . معه خفية. قيد بما ذكر لأنه لا يلزمه اجتناب ميل قلبه إلى أحدهما لأنه ليس في وسعه كالقسم. وفي الولوالجية ولا ينبغي للذي يقوم بين يدي القاضي أن يسار أحداً . من الخصمين في مجلس الحكم لأنه نائب القاضي اهـ. وأما منعه من ضيافة أحدهما فما رواه الحسن فقال : جاء رجل فنزل على علي رضي الله عنه فأضافه فلما فرغ قال : أني أريد أن أخاصم قال له : تحول فإن النبي ل نهانا أن نضيف الخصم إلا ومعه خصمه، قيد بضيافة أحدهما لأن له أن يضيفهما معاً لما رويناه قوله : (والمزاح) أي وليتق المزاح في المصباح : مزح مزحاً من باب نفع ومزاحة بالفتح ، والاسم المزاح بالضم وهو الدعابة، والمزاحة المرة ومازحت مزاحاً من باب قاتل قتالاً .اهـ. وفي الصحاح : الدعابة بالضم المزاح من دعب لعب اهـ. فعلى هذا المزاح اللعب. وأشار إلى أنه لا يضحك في وجه أحدهما فلا يقوم له إذا قدم بالأولى فلو قال المصنف والمزح» لكان أولى لأنه يجتنب المزح سواء مازحه أحد أو لا، وسواء كان مع أحد الخصمين أو مع غيرهما. ومراده إذا كان في مجلس الحكم، وأما في غيره فلا يكثر منه لأنه يذهب بالمهابة قوله : وتلقين (الشاهد) أي يجتنبه لأن فيه إعانة لأحدهما على الآخر. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في موضع تهمة أو لا، واستحسنه أبو يوسف في غير موضع التهمه لأنه قد يقول أعلم مكان أشهد لمهابة المجلس وهو نوع رخصة عنده رجع إليه بعدما تولى القضاء والعزيمة فيما قالا لأنه لا يخلو عن نوع تهمة. وفي فتح القدير : وظاهر الجواب ترجيح ما عن أبي يوسف. وفي القنية من باب المفتي : والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف فيما يتعلق بالقضاء لزيادة تجربته، وكذا في البزازية من القضاء والتلقين أن يقول له القاضي كلاماً يستفيد به علماً. وذكر الصدر أن منه أن يقول له كيف تشهد وإنما بم تشهد . وأما إفتاء القاضي فالصحيح أنه لا بأس به في مجلس القضاء وغيره لكن لا يفتي أحد الخصمين؛ كذا في خزانة الفتاوى. وفي الملتقط : فأما اليوم فقد ظهرت المذاهب إلا إذا كانت مسألة لا يعرف جوابها في مذهب القاضي .اهـ. قيد بالشاهد لبيان أنه لا يلقز المدعي بالأولى. وفي الخانية : ولو أمر القاضي رجلين ليعلماه الدعوى والخصومة فلا بأس به خصوصاً على قول أبي يوسف.
His saying: 'And let him avoid whispering to one of them, gesturing to him, prompting his evidence, and hosting him' meaning let him avoid these things because they involve suspicion and breaking the heart of the other. Whispering is speaking secretly in the ear; as in al-Qamus. The meaning is that he avoids speaking with him secretly. It is restricted to what was mentioned because it is not obligatory for him to avoid inclination of his heart towards one of them as it is not within his capacity, like division. In al-Walwaljiyya: It is not appropriate for the one who stands before the judge to whisper to either litigant in the session because he is the judge's deputy. As for the prohibition of hosting one of them, it is based on what al-Hasan narrated: A man came and stayed with Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), and he hosted him. When he finished, he said: 'I want to sue.' Ali said: 'Move away, for the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade us from hosting a litigant unless his opponent is with him.' It is restricted to hosting one of them because he may host both together as we narrated. His saying: (and jesting) i.e., let him avoid jesting. In al-Misbah: mazaha, mazhan, from the pattern nafa'a, and mizahun, and the noun al-mizah is joking, and al-mazah is the instance, and mazahatu mizahan from the pattern qatala qitalan. In al-Sihah: al-du'abah is joking, from da'iba la'iba. So on this, jesting is playing. He indicated that he should not laugh in the face of one of them, so he should not stand for him when he comes, a fortiori. If the author had said 'al-mazh' it would have been better because he avoids jesting whether one jests with him or not, and whether it is with one of the litigants or with others. His intent is when in the session of judgment; as for outside, he should not do it excessively because it removes awe. His saying: (and prompting the witness) i.e., he avoids it because it involves assisting one over the other. He left it general, including whether in a place of suspicion or not. Abu Yusuf considered it good in other than a place of suspicion because the witness might say 'I know the place of testimony' due to the awe of the court, and it is a kind of concession on his part that he returned to after becoming judge. The determination according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that it is not free of some suspicion. In Fath al-Qadir: The apparent answer is a preference for what is from Abu Yusuf. In al-Qunyah, from the chapter of the mufti: The fatwa is on the statement of Abu Yusuf regarding matters of judiciary due to his increased experience. Similarly in al-Bazzaziyyah from judiciary: Prompting is that the judge says something to him by which he gains knowledge. Al-Sadr mentioned that it includes him saying to him: 'How do you testify?' and 'What do you testify as to?' As for the judge giving a legal opinion, the correct view is that it is permissible in the session of judgment and elsewhere, but he should not give an opinion to one of the litigants; as in Khizanat al-Fatawa. In al-Multaqat: As for today, the schools have become apparent unless it is an issue whose answer is not known in the judge's school. He restricted it to the witness to clarify that he does not prompt the plaintiff, a fortiori. In al-Khaniyyah: If the judge orders two men to teach him the claim and the dispute, there is no harm, especially according to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on what a judge must avoid in court. The author explains the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq which lists five prohibitions: whispering (musararah), gesturing (isharah), prompting evidence (talqin hujjah), hosting (diyafah), and jesting (mizah). Each is elaborated with lexical definitions and legal reasoning. The ruling is based on preventing suspicion and maintaining impartiality. The hadith of Ali (RA) is cited as proof for hosting: a judge must not host one litigant without the other. For prompting the witness, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf allows it outside of suspicion, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) prohibit it absolutely. The later fatwa, according to the texts cited (Fath al-Qadir, al-Qunyah), prefers Abu Yusuf's view due to his judicial experience. However, the author notes that the determination ('azimah) is with the two. The passage concludes with further rulings, such as the judge appointing assistants to learn the claim, which is allowed, and that the judge may give general legal opinions but not to a single litigant.

Key Terms
مسارة (musararah): Whispering to one litigant in secret, which could create suspicion.
تلقين الشاهد (talqin al-shahid): Prompting the witness by the judge, i.e., suggesting words or guiding his testimony.
ضيافة (diyafah): Hospitality, specifically hosting one litigant without the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a judge hosting one litigant without the other?
A) Permissible if the judge remains impartial
B) Prohibited based on the hadith of Ali
C) Recommended to build rapport
D) Only prohibited if the litigant is wealthy

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid any action that creates the appearance of partiality, even if his heart is pure, and must follow the mufta bihi ruling on prompting witnesses.

Imprisonment in Hanafi Law: Definition, Proofs, and Conditions
Definition, proofs, historical origin, and rules of imprisonment by a judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition and legal basis of imprisonment (habs) in the Hanafi school, identify its conditions, and apply the exception for funeral attendance.

Core Idea
Imprisonment is a legitimate judicial tool with humane conditions; release for a funeral is only for close kin with a guarantor.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines imprisonment (habs) as a judge's tool against those refusing to pay debts or as a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). It presents the Quranic and Sunnah evidence, traces the historical origin of prisons in Islam, and lays out the strict physical and procedural conditions that make imprisonment both effective and just. A notable intra-Hanafi difference arises over whether a prisoner may attend a funeral with a guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that imprisonment (habs) is a discretionary power of the judge (ja'iz), not a mandatory punishment. It applies to the mumtani' (one who refuses to pay a right) and as ta'zir.
2
Locate the dalil
The proofs are Quran (5:33, 'or they be exiled' interpreted as imprisonment), Sunnah (the Prophet imprisoned a man on suspicion), and Ijma.
The Quranic dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala; the Sunnah is ahad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Quranic word 'nufy' (exile) is interpreted as imprisonment because both achieve prevention; the hadith shows direct practice. Understand how the Hanafi school derives the ruling from these texts.
4
Examine the historical development
Imprisonment used to be in the mosque until 'Ali (RA) built the first prison (Nafi'), then a stronger one (Muhayyas). This shows the evolution of institutional detention.
The poem by 'Ali emphasizes security and trust.
5
Analyze the conditions and exceptions
The prison must have no bedding; only relatives and neighbors may visit briefly; the prisoner may not leave for prayer, Hajj, or funeral except for close relatives (parents, grandparents, children) with a guarantor, per al-Khulasah. Note the critique in Fath al-Qadir and the reconciliation by distinguishing debtor vs guarantor.
The mufta bihi is al-Khulasah's allowance for funeral attendance of specified close kin.

Source Text

فصل في الحبس
قدمنا أنه مما يملكه القاضي على الممتنع عن إيفاء الحق وتعزيراً فكان من عمله فذكره فيه. وهو في اللغة المنع وهو مصدر حبسه من باب ضرب ثم أطلق على الموضع وجمع على حبوس مثل فلس وفلوس كذا في المصباح. ودليله الكتاب أو ينفوا من [المائدة : 33] والمراد منه الحبس والسنة حبسه عليه الصلاة والسلام رجلاً بالتهمة. والإجماع عليه وكان في المسجد إلى زمن علي رضي الله عنه فبنى سجناً وهو أول من بناه في الإسلام وسماه نافعاً ولم يكن حصيناً لكونه من قصب فانفلت الناس منه فبنى آخر وسماه محيساً وكان من مدر وفي ذلك يقول علي : ألا تراني كيساً مكيساً بنيت بعد نافع

Chapter on Imprisonment
We have already presented that it is something the judge has authority over against the one who refuses to fulfill a right, and as a discretionary punishment, so it is part of his work, and he mentioned it here. Linguistically, it means prevention; it is the masdar of habasahu from the pattern daraba, then it was used for the place, and its plural is hubus like filos and fulus; as in al-Misbah. Its evidence is the Book: 'or they be exiled from the land' [al-Maidah 5:33] and the intended meaning is imprisonment; and the Sunnah: he (peace be upon him) imprisoned a man on suspicion. And there is consensus upon it. It used to be in the mosque until the time of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), so he built a prison, and he was the first to build one in Islam, and he named it Nafi', but it was not fortified because it was made of reeds, so people escaped from it, so he built another and named it Muhayyas, and it was made of clay. In that regard, Ali said: 'Do you not see me clever, most clever? After Nafi', I built a fortified door and a trustworthy clever one.' In another narration: 'a fortified fortress', and in another: 'I replaced' instead of 'I built', and in another: 'a strong door', and in another: 'a commander' instead of 'trustworthy'. Al-Muhayyas (with a khā' and a tā' marbūṭah) is the place of takhyeeb (humiliation) and it is subduing; it is also narrated with a kasrah on the yā' because it subdues whoever falls into it. Al-Kays is good deliberation in matters; and al-kays is attributed to the well-known kays (money bag); and 'trustworthy' he intended, and he set up 'trustworthy' meaning the jailer, as in his saying: 'girding a sword and a spear'; as in al-Fa'iq. The description of a prison is that it should be in a place where there is no bedding or carpet, and no one can enter it for companionship except his relatives and neighbors, and they do not stay, and he does not go out for Friday prayer, nor for congregation, nor for obligatory Hajj, nor for attending a funeral, even with a guarantor. In al-Khulasah: He goes out with a guarantor for the funeral of parents, grandfathers, grandmothers, and children, but for others he does not go out, and upon that is the fatwa. It was criticized in Fath al-Qadir because Muhammad explicitly stated the opposite, but it may be refuted by saying that Muhammad's statement is about the original debtor, while the discussion here is about the guarantor. Nor for the advent of Ramadan and the two Eids to dishearten his heart and he pays. Nor for the death of his relative unless there is no one to wash and shroud him, then he goes out due to the closeness of birth. And if he falls ill with a debilitating illness, then if there is someone
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on imprisonment (habs) by the judge. It begins by stating that imprisonment is a tool the judge may use against someone who refuses to pay a debt (mumtani') or as a discretionary punishment (ta'zir). The linguistic definition is 'prevention,' and the plural is hubus. The proofs are from the Quran (5:33, where 'exile' is interpreted as imprisonment by some exegetes), the Sunnah (the Prophet imprisoned a man on suspicion), and consensus (ijma'). Historically, the first prison in Islam was built by 'Ali (RA) after prisoners escaped from the mosque. The text then describes the proper conditions of a prison: it should be bare (no bedding), only relatives and neighbors may visit briefly, and the prisoner may not leave for Friday prayer, congregation, or Hajj, nor even for a funeral unless by a specific exception in al-Khulasah (parents, grandparents, children). The author notes a disagreement: Fath al-Qadir critiques al-Khulasah's allowance for funeral attendance, arguing that Muhammad's (Shaybani) text contradicts it, but this is reconciled by distinguishing between the original debtor and the guarantor. Finally, the passage lists additional restrictions: no release for Ramadan or 'Eid, and for a relative's death only if there is no one else to wash and shroud. The text cuts off mid-sentence discussing illness.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment; the judge's act of detaining a person who refuses to fulfill a right or as ta'zir.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment determined by the judge for offenses without fixed hadd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Surah al-Hujurat verse 9 about reconciliation

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment in Hanafi law is a discretionary, condition-bound tool for judges, with specific humane requirements and a carefully defined exception for attending close relatives' funerals.

Imprisonment for Debt in Hanafi Law
Explains ruling on imprisoning a debtor and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on imprisoning a debtor, identify which debts qualify (badal maal), and describe the procedure and conditions the judge must follow.

Core Idea
Only property debts justify imprisonment; the judge must first ask and the debtor's dignity is protected.

This lesson explores the Hanafi position on imprisoning a debtor who can pay but refuses. The ruling is not automatic — only specific debts qualify, and the judge must follow a careful procedure. We will also examine the ethical treatment of the imprisoned debtor and the scholarly differences within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Imprisonment of a debtor is a permissible (ja'iz) coercive measure for the judge when the debtor is able to pay but refuses. It is not a fixed punishment but a ta'zir.
The ruling applies specifically to debts that are 'badal maal' (substitute for property).
2
Determine which debts qualify
The passage lists four categories: price (thaman), loan (qard), prompt dower (mahr mu'ajjal), and what one undertakes by suretyship (kafalah). These are all considered property debts.
Other obligations like wages (ujrah) or maintenance (nafaqah) are generally not subject to imprisonment unless they fall under the same category.
3
Trace the judge's procedure
When a creditor proves the debt, the judge first orders the debtor to pay. If the debtor claims poverty, the creditor must prove wealth. If the debtor admits to having wealth but refuses, only then does the judge imprison.
The presence of the opponent is not a condition for the judge to proceed.
4
Examine the debtor's treatment
The imprisoned debtor must not be beaten, shackled, collared, stripped, hired out, or humiliated. He may be allowed conjugal visits and given opportunity to earn if possible.
If escape is feared, the judge may transfer to a more secure prison or use shackles.
5
Note intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage records disagreements: Abu Yusuf allows hiring out the debtor; Sarakhsi does not expedite imprisonment for proof by evidence. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that imprisonment is done after refusal and expedited if proven by evidence.
Mark the arbitrator (muhakkam) vs. appointed judge (qadi): only the qadi may order imprisonment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء
المعجل وما التزمه بالكفالة لا في غيره إن ادعى الفقر إلا أن يثبت غريمه غناه فيحبسه بما رأى يخدمه لا يخرج وإلا أخرج بكفيل وإلا لا يطلقه وحضرة الخصم ليست شرطاً. ولا يخرج للمعالجة لإمكانها في السجن، ولا يمنع من الجماع إن احتاج إليه فتدخل امرأته أو جاريته عليه إن كان فيه موضع سترة. واختلفوا في منعه من الكسب والأصح المنع؛ كذا في الخلاصة. ولا يضرب المديون ولا يقيد ولا يغل ولا يجرد ولا يؤاجر ولا يقام بين يدي صاحب الحق إهانة. وفي المنتقى : إذا خاف فراره قيده؛ كذا في البزازية. وفيها : إذا خيف أنه يفر من السجن يحول إلى سجن اللصوص. وإذا جلس المحبوس في السجن متعنتاً لا يوفي المال قال الإمام الأرسانيدي: يطين الباب ويترك له ثقبة يلقي منها الماء والخبز. وقال القاضي : الرأي فيه إلى القاضي اهـ. وفي الخانية : إذا كان للمحبوس ديون على الناس فإن القاضي يخرجه ليخاصم ثم يحبس .اهـ. وصرحوا في كتاب الظهار أنه إذا امتنع من التكفير قدرته يضرب، وصرحوا في كتاب النفقات أنه لو امتنع من الإنفاق على قريبه يضرب بخلاف سائر الديون اهـ. وعن أبي يوسف أن القاضي يؤجره لقضاء دينه وعليه حمل ما في الحديث من أنه باع حراً في دينه أي أجره وتعيين مكان الحبس للقاضي إلا إذا طلب المدعي مكاناً آخر لما في القنية : ادعى على بنته مالاً وأمر القاضي بحبسها فطلب الأب منه أن يحبسها في موضع آخر غير السجن حتى لا يضيع عرضه يجيبه القاضي إلى ذلك، وكذا في كل مدع مع المدعى عليه اهـ. وفي المحيط : ويجعل للنساء سجن على حدة نفياً لوقوع الفتنة .
قوله : (وإذا ثبت الحق للمدعي أمره بدفع ما عليه فإن أبى حبسه في الثمن والقرض والمهر المعجل وما التزمه بالكفالة لأنه جزاء الظلم وقد صار ضالماً بمنعه أطلقه وقيده في الهداية بالقاضي فظاهره أن المحكم لا يحبس ولم أره الآن صريحاً. أطلق الثبوت فشمل ما إذا كان ببينة أو بإقرار وفرق بينهما في الهداية بأنه إذا ثبت بالبينة عجل حبسه لظهور المطل بإنكاره وإلا لم يعجل فإذا امتنع حبسه وهو المذهب عندنا وعكسه شمس الأئمة السرخسي
هنا في النهر والمنح قوله : (ولا وطاء) قال في المصباح : الوطاء وزان كتاب المهاد الوطئ وقد وطق الفراش بالضم فهو وطئ مثل قرب فهو قريب .اهـ. وقال في مختار الصحاح : والمهاد الفراش ومهد الفراش بسطه ووطأه وبابه قطع قوله : (وقد يدفع بأن نص محمد الخ) قال في النهر: هذا سهو وذلك أنه نقل في الخلاصة يخرج بالكفيل فسقطت الباء في نسخته .اهـ. وذكر نحوه الرملي ثم قال : والعجب أن البزازي وقع في ذلك فقال : وذكر القاضي أن الكفيل يخرج الجنازة الوالدين الخ والذي في فتاوى القاضي يخرج بالكفيل قوله : (فظاهره أن المحكم لا يحبس كذا قال في النهر أيضاً. وفي حاشية أبي السعود عن الحموي : صرح صدر الشريعة بأن المحكم يحبس قوله : (وهو المذهب عندنا) كذا قاله في شرح ادب القضاء أيضا وذكر أن التسوية بينهما في انه لا يحبسه في " وتمامه في شرح أدب القضاء للخصاف والأحسن إطلاق الكتاب من الأمر بالإيفاء مطلقاً فلا يعجل
أول وهله قوله :

لأنه إذا ثبت بالبينة ربما تعلل بأنه لم يعلم به إلا الآن وقد فرق الحلواني بين ما ثبت بالبينة أنه فيخبره القاضي يريد القضاء ويقول ألك مخرج، وبين ما ثبت بالإقرار فلا يعلمه، وتمامه في شرح أدب القضاء للخصاف والأحسن إطلاق الكتاب من الأمر بالإيفاء مطلقاً فلا يعجل بحبسه وذكر الشارح أن الصواب أنه لا يحبسه حتى يسأله فإن أقر أن له مالاً أمره بالدفع، فإن أبى حبسه وإلا سأل المدعي عن البينة أن له مالاً، فإن برهن أمره بالدفع، فإن أبي حبسه، وإن عجز واختلفا فالقول للمدعي في الأشياء الأربعة وللمدعى عليه في غيرها اهـ. ونقله في البناية عن الخصاف وهو خلاف المذهب ولكن يسأل المدعي عن ماله إذا طلب المديون إجماعاً ؛ كذا في شرح الصدر أطلق الحق فشمل القليل والكثير ولو دائقاً وهو سدس درهم، ولو قال حبسه بطلب المدعي لكان أولى كما ذكره قاضيخان وقال شريح: يحبسه من غير طلبه؛ كذا في البناية ولو قال المديون أبيع عرضي وأقضي ديني أجله القاضي ثلاثة ولا يحبسه ولو له عقار يحبسه ليبيعه ويقضي الدين ولو بثمن قليل وإن وجد المديون من يقرضه ليقضي به دينه فلم يفعل فهو ظالم؛ كذا في البزازية وفي كراهية القنية : ولو كان للمديون حرفة تفضي إلى قضاء دينه فامتنع منها لا يعذر .اهـ. وأطلق الثمن فشمل الأجرة الواجبة لأنها ثمن المنافع، وشمل ما على المشتري وما على البائع بعد فسخ البيع بينهما بإقالة أو خيار، وشمل رأس مال السلم بعد الإقالة وما إذا قبض المشتري المبيع أو لا، ولا شك في دخول الأجرة تحت قولهم أو التزمه بعقد إن لم تجعل ثمن المنافع ويتفاوت الحال، فإن دخلت تحت ما كان بدل مال حبسه عليها على فتوى قاضيخان أيضاً وإلا لم يحبس عليها على ما أفتى به، ولم أر من صرح بها لكن لم يذكر المؤلف حبسه على العين المغصوبة هنا، وذكره في كتاب الغصب بنفي الأمانات إذا استنع الأمين من دفعها غير مدع لهلاكها فإنه يحبس عليها وصارت مغصوبة، وما في تهذيب القلانسي وهو إذا ثبت الحق بإقرار أو بحكم بنكوله أو ببينة فمطل المطلوب عن تسليمه وطلب الطالب حبسه أمره بحبسه في كل عين يقدر على تسليمها وفي كل دين لزمه بدلاً عن مال كثمن المبيع وبدل القرض والمغصوب ونحوه أو بالتزامه بعقد كالمهر والكفالة أهـ. أولى كما لا يخفى ولشموله الحكم بالنكول بخلاف من قيد ثبوت الحق بالبينة أو الإقرار. وأشار المؤلف إلى حبس الكفيل والأصيل معاً الكفيل بما التزمه والأصيل بما لزمه بدلاً عن مال وللكفيل بالأمر حبس الأصيل إذا حبس؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي
بحبسه، وذكر الشارح أن الصواب أنه لا يحسبه كذا في بعض النسخ وفي بعضها وتمامه في شرح أدب القضاء للخصاف أنه لا يحبسه وعليها كتب الرملي مستشكلاً لها وقد علمت ما فيها من السقط». قوله: (ولكن يسأل المدعي عن ماله الخ) قال الرملي : يعني أن يسأل المديون من القاضي أن يسأل صاحب الدين أله مال سأله القاضي بالإجماع اهـ قلت وسيأتي في أثناء القولة الآتية لو قال
Book of Judicial Rulings. The prompt [dower] and what one undertakes by suretyship [are subject to imprisonment], but not other [debts] if he claims poverty, unless his creditor proves his wealth, then he imprisons him as he sees fit. He is not released for service; otherwise he is released with a surety; otherwise he does not release him. The presence of the opponent is not a condition. He is not released for treatment because it is possible in prison. He is not prevented from intercourse if he needs it, so his wife or his concubine may enter upon him if there is a place of concealment. They differed about preventing him from earning; the correct view is prevention—as in al-Khulasa. The debtor is not beaten, shackled, collared, stripped, hired out, nor stood before the creditor as humiliation. In al-Muntaqa: if his escape is feared, he is shackled—as in al-Bazzaziyya. In it: if it is feared he will flee from prison, he is transferred to the thieves' prison. When the imprisoned person sits stubbornly in prison without paying the money, Imam al-Arsanidi said: the door is plastered and a hole is left for him through which water and bread are thrown. Al-Qadi said: the opinion is left to the judge. In al-Khaniyya: if the imprisoned person has debts owed to him by people, the judge releases him to litigate and then imprisons him. They stated in the book of Zihar that if he refrains from expiation, he is beaten. They stated in the book of Nafaqat that if he refrains from spending on his relative, he is beaten, contrary to other debts. From Abu Yusuf: the judge hires him out to pay his debt, and upon this is carried the hadith that he sold a free man for his debt—i.e., hired him out. The location of imprisonment is for the judge, except if the plaintiff requests another place, as in al-Quniyya: a man claimed money from his daughter and the judge ordered her imprisonment; the father asked him to imprison her in a place other than the prison so that her honor not be lost; the judge grants that, and similarly for every claimant and defendant. In al-Muhit: a separate prison is made for women to prevent fitna. His statement: (When the right is proven to the plaintiff, he orders him to pay what is due; if he refuses, he imprisons him in [cases of] price, loan, prompt dower, and what he undertook by suretyship, because it is the recompense for oppression and he has become an oppressor by withholding it.) He released it [i.e., the general ruling] and restricted it in al-Hidaya to the [main] judge, so apparently the arbitrator does not imprison—I have not seen it explicitly now. He made the proof absolute, so it includes whether by evidence or admission; al-Hidaya differentiates: if proven by evidence, his imprisonment is expedited due to apparent delay by his denial; otherwise it is not expedited; if he refuses, he is imprisoned—this is our school, contrary to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. Here in al-Nahr and al-Minh. His statement: (and not for service) ... [continues with linguistic notes]. His statement: (and it may be refuted by the text of Muhammad...) said in al-Nahr: this is an oversight; ... The correct view is that he is not imprisoned until he is asked; if he admits he has money, he is ordered to pay; if he refuses, he is imprisoned; otherwise the plaintiff is asked for evidence that he has money; if he proves it, he is ordered to pay; if he refuses, he is imprisoned; if he cannot [prove] and they differ, the plaintiff's statement is taken in the four things [i.e., price, loan, dower, suretyship] and the defendant's in others. This is quoted in al-Binaya from al-Khassaf, but it is against the school; however, the plaintiff is asked about his money if the debtor requests—by consensus, as in Sharh al-Sadr. The right is made absolute, so it includes little and much, even one daaniq (1/6 dirham). If he said imprisonment upon the plaintiff's request, it would be better—as Qadikhan said; Shuraih said: he imprisons without his request—as in al-Binaya. If the debtor says: 'I will sell my goods and pay my debt,' the judge gives him three days and does not imprison him. If he has real estate, he imprisons him so he can sell it and pay the debt, even for a low price. If the debtor finds someone who will lend him money to pay his debt but he does not do so, he is an oppressor—as in al-Bazzaziyya. In Karihat al-Quniyya: if the debtor has a profession that would lead to paying his debt but refrains, he is not excused. The [term] price is made absolute, so it includes the obligatory wage because it is the price of benefits; it includes what is owed by the buyer and what is owed by the seller after rescission of a sale by revocation or option; it includes the capital of salam after revocation and whether the buyer has taken possession of the sold item or not. There is no doubt that the wage is included under 'or what he undertook by contract' if it is not made the price of benefits, and the situation differs: if it falls under what is a substitute for property, he is imprisoned for it according to Qadikhan's fatwa; otherwise he is not imprisoned for it according to what he gave fatwa on. I have not seen anyone explicitly state it, but the author did not mention imprisonment for the usurped object here, and he mentioned it in the book of Ghasb: including trusts, if the trustee refrains from handing them over without claiming they perished, he is imprisoned for them and they become usurped. What is in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: when the right is proven by admission, or by a ruling due to default, or by evidence, and the defendant delays its delivery and the plaintiff requests his imprisonment, he orders his imprisonment for every tangible thing he is able to deliver and for every debt that is due to him as a substitute for property, like the price of a sold item, compensation for a loan, a usurped object, and the like, or by undertaking a contract like dower and suretyship. It is more appropriate, as is clear, and because it includes a ruling by default, unlike those who restrict the proving of the right to evidence or admission. The author indicated the imprisonment of both the surety and the principal: the surety for what he undertook, and the principal for what is due from him as a substitute for property; the surety by order may imprison the principal if he is imprisoned—as in al-Muhit. And in [the continuation]: 'with his imprisonment' and the commentator stated that the correct view is that he does not imprison him—as in some copies, and in others the completion in Sharh Adab al-Qada' al-Khassaf is that he does not imprison him, and upon this al-Ramli wrote, questioning it, and you have known what omission it contains. His statement: (but the plaintiff is asked about his money, etc.) al-Ramli said: meaning that the debtor asks the judge to ask the creditor whether he has money; the judge asks him by consensus. I say: and it will come later in the following statement, 'if he said'.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the Hanafi ruling on imprisoning a debtor. The author, after stating the base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expands on the conditions for imprisonment. The core ruling: a debtor who is able to pay but refuses may be imprisoned by the judge, but only for specific types of debts: the price (thaman) of a sale, loan (qard), prompt dower (mahr mu'ajjal), and what one undertakes by suretyship (kafalah). These are termed 'substitute for property' (badal maal). Other obligations, like unpaid wages or maintenance, are generally not subject to imprisonment unless they fall under the same category. The passage also discusses the procedure: the judge must first ask the debtor about his financial state; if he admits to having wealth, he is ordered to pay; if he refuses, he is imprisoned. If he claims poverty, the plaintiff must prove his wealth. If the debtor has property, he may be given a grace period to sell it. The text also addresses the treatment of the imprisoned debtor: he should not be beaten, shackled, or humiliated; he may be allowed conjugal visits if there is privacy; he may be transferred to a more secure prison if escape is feared; and in extreme cases of stubborn refusal, the door may be sealed with only a hole for food and water—though this is a minority view. For women, a separate prison is established to prevent fitna. An important point: the arbitrator (muhakkam) does not have the power to imprison—only the appointed judge (qadi) does, according to the relied-upon position. The passage also clarifies that imprisonment is expedited if the debt is proven by clear evidence (bayyinah) because the debtor's denial indicates deliberate delay, whereas if proven by admission (iqrar), the debtor may be given leeway. The author resolves a textual issue regarding whether the surety (kafil) can also be imprisoned, confirming that both the surety and principal can be imprisoned. Overall, this passage demonstrates the Hanafi school's balanced approach: it enforces debt payment to prevent oppression but also protects the debtor's dignity and basic rights.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): Imprisonment ordered by the judge for a debtor who is able to pay but refuses, as a coercive measure.
بدل مال (badal maal): A substitute for property, i.e., a debt that originates as compensation for property, such as price, loan, or dower.
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
المطل (al-matal): Delay or procrastination in paying a debt despite being able to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following debts is subject to imprisonment of the debtor who can pay but refuses?
A) Unpaid wages for work
B) Mahr mu'ajjal (prompt dower)
C) Unpaid rent (ijarah)
D) Maintenance for a relative

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment for debt in Hanafi law is a discretionary coercive measure limited to property debts (badal maal), requiring the judge to first ask the debtor and protecting the debtor's dignity.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
12 min
Imprisonment for Debt: Scope and Procedure
Ruling with proof on imprisonment for debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a debtor may be imprisoned in Hanafi fiqh, apply the distinction between badal mal and non-badal mal debts, and explain the judge's procedural steps.

Core Idea
Imprisonment for debt is restricted to debts that are substitutes for property; not all debts qualify.

The Hanafi school carefully limits the use of imprisonment as a coercive measure for debt. This lesson unpacks the key ruling from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, clarifying which debts trigger imprisonment, the judge's procedure, and the important distinction between debts that are a substitute for property (badal mal) and personal obligations like dower or guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hukm and its conditions
The text establishes that imprisonment for debt is permissible (or obligatory) under specific conditions. You must identify those conditions: the debt must be a substitute for property, the debtor must be able to pay but refuse, and the claimant must request imprisonment.
Later fatwa adds that the judge asks the claimant only if the debtor requests it.
2
Distinguish between bayyinah and iqrar
Al-Halwani's distinction: when the debt is proven by evidence (bayyinah), the judge informs the debtor and offers a chance; when proven by confession (iqrar), no information is needed. This affects the procedure but not the final ruling.
The commentary states the correct view is not to rush to prison.
3
Analyze the scope of 'badal mal'
The text expands 'thaman' (price) to include wages, amounts after rescission, salam capital, etc. You must distinguish between debts that are substitutes for property (imprisonment allowed) and those that are not, like dower and guarantee (not allowed per later fatwa).
Imprisonment for usurped property (maghsub) is also included.
4
Examine the dispute on preliminary questioning
There is a disagreement (al-Khassaf vs. the madhhab) on whether the judge should ask the claimant about the debtor's assets initially. The relied-upon position is that the judge does not ask unless the debtor requests.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with a clear thamarah.
5
Apply the ruling to concrete scenarios
Given a case, determine if imprisonment is warranted based on the debt type, the debtor's ability, and procedural steps. For example, a debtor who refuses to pay the price of a sold item can be imprisoned; one who owes dower cannot.
The debtor's claim of poverty is accepted for non-badal mal debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء ٤٧٧ 6 . لأنه إذا ثبت بالبينة ربما تعلل بأنه لم يعلم به إلا الآن وقد فرق الحلواني بين ما ثبت بالبينة أنه فيخبره القاضي يريد القضاء ويقول ألك مخرج، وبين ما ثبت بالإقرار فلا يعلمه، وتمامه في شرح أدب القضاء للخصاف والأحسن إطلاق الكتاب من الأمر بالإيفاء مطلقاً فلا يعجل بحبسه وذكر الشارح أن الصواب أنه لا يحبسه حتى يسأله فإن أقر أن له مالاً أمره بالدفع، فإن أبى حبسه وإلا سأل المدعي عن البينة أن له مالاً، فإن برهن أمره بالدفع، فإن أبي حبسه، وإن عجز واختلفا فالقول للمدعي في الأشياء الأربعة وللمدعى عليه في غيرها اهـ. ونقله في البناية عن الخصاف وهو خلاف المذهب ولكن يسأل المدعي عن ماله إذا طلب المديون إجماعاً ؛ كذا في شرح الصدر أطلق الحق فشمل القليل والكثير ولو دائقاً وهو سدس درهم، ولو قال حبسه بطلب المدعي لكان أولى كما ذكره قاضيخان وقال شريح: يحبسه من غير طلبه ؛ كذا في البناية ولو قال المديون أبيع عرضي وأقضي ديني أجله القاضي ثلاثة ولا يحبسه ولو له عقار يحبسه ليبيعه ويقضي الدين ولو بثمن قليل وإن وجد المديون من يقرضه ليقضي به دينه فلم يفعل فهو ظالم ؛ كذا في البزازية وفي كراهية القنية : ولو كان للمديون حرفة تفضي إلى قضاء دينه فامتنع منها لا يعذر .اهـ. وأطلق الثمن فشمل الأجرة الواجبة لأنها ثمن المنافع، وشمل ما على المشتري وما على البائع بعد فسخ البيع بينهما بإقالة أو خيار، وشمل رأس مال السلم بعد الإقالة وما إذا قبض المشتري المبيع أو لا، ولا شك في دخول الأجرة تحت قولهم أو التزمه بعقد إن لم تجعل ثمن المنافع ويتفاوت الحال، فإن دخلت تحت ما كان بدل مال حبسه عليها على فتوى قاضيخان أيضاً وإلا لم يحبس عليها على ما أفتى به، ولم أر من صرح بها لكن لم يذكر المؤلف حبسه على العين المغصوبة هنا، وذكره في كتاب الغصب بنفي الأمانات إذا استنع الأمين من دفعها غير مدع لهلاكها فإنه يحبس عليها وصارت مغصوبة، وما في تهذيب القلانسي وهو إذا ثبت الحق بإقرار أو بحكم بنكوله أو ببينة فمطل المطلوب عن تسليمه وطلب الطالب حبسه أمره بحبسه في كل عين يقدر على تسليمها وفي كل دين لزمه بدلاً عن مال كثمن المبيع وبدل القرض والمغصوب ونحوه أو بالتزامه بعقد كالمهر والكفالة أهـ. أولى كما لا يخفى ولشموله الحكم بالنكول بخلاف من قيد ثبوت الحق بالبينة أو الإقرار.
Chapter of Judgments. Because if it is proven by evidence, he might excuse himself by saying he did not know until now, and al-Halwani distinguished between what is proven by evidence – the judge informs him, intends to judge, and says 'Do you have a way out?' – and what is proven by confession – he does not inform him. The complete discussion is in Sharh Adab al-Qada' for al-Khassaf. The best is to release the book from the command to pay absolutely, so he should not rush to imprison him. The commentator mentioned that the correct view is that he does not imprison him until he asks him. If he admits he has money, he orders him to pay; if he refuses, he imprisons him. Otherwise, he asks the claimant for evidence that he has money. If he proves it, he orders him to pay; if he refuses, he imprisons him. If he is unable and they differ, the claimant's word is taken in four things, and the defendant's word in other things. This was transmitted in al-Binaya from al-Khassaf, and it contradicts the madhhab, but the claimant is asked about his property if the debtor requests, by consensus – as in Sharh al-Sadr. He made the right absolute, covering little and much, even a daaniq, which is a sixth of a dirham. If he said his imprisonment is at the claimant's request, it would be better, as Qadikhan mentioned. Shuraih said: He imprisons him without his request – as in al-Binaya. If the debtor says 'I will sell my goods and pay my debt', the judge gives him three days and does not imprison him. If he has real estate, he imprisons him to sell it and pay the debt, even at a low price. If the debtor finds someone who would lend him to pay his debt but does not do so, he is unjust – as in al-Bazzaziyya. In Karahiyya al-Quniyya: If the debtor has a profession that would lead to paying his debt but refrains, he is not excused. The price (thaman) is unrestricted, so it includes the obligatory wage because it is the price of benefits; it includes what is owed by the buyer and the seller after rescission of the sale by revocation or option; it includes the capital of salam after revocation, whether the buyer took possession or not. There is no doubt that the wage falls under their statement 'or he undertook it by contract' if it is not considered the price of benefits, and the situation varies. If it falls under what is a substitute for property, he is imprisoned for it according to Qadikhan's fatwa; otherwise, he is not imprisoned for it according to the fatwa. I have not seen anyone explicitly state this, but the author did not mention imprisonment for usurped property here; he mentioned it in the Book of Usurpation when excluding trusts: if the trustee refuses to hand over the property without claiming it perished, he is imprisoned for it and it becomes usurped. What is in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: If the right is established by confession, or by judgment due to his refusal, or by evidence, and the obligated party delays delivery and the claimant requests his imprisonment, he orders his imprisonment for every tangible thing he can deliver and for every debt that is a substitute for property, like the price of a sale, the substitute for a loan, usurped property, etc., or what he undertook by contract like dower and guarantee. This is more appropriate as it is not hidden, and it includes judgment due to refusal, contrary to those who restrict the establishment of the right to evidence or confession.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the imprisonment of a debtor for debt, with a focus on the judge's procedure and the types of debts that warrant imprisonment. The text begins by discussing a distinction made by al-Halwani: when a debt is proven by evidence (bayyinah), the judge should inform the debtor and ask if he has a way to pay, but when proven by confession (iqrar), the judge does not need to inform him. The commentator (sharih) states that the correct position is that the judge should not rush to imprison the debtor; rather, he should first ask him. If the debtor admits he has wealth, the judge orders him to pay; if he refuses, he is imprisoned. If the debtor denies having wealth, the judge asks the claimant for evidence that the debtor has money. If the claimant proves it, the debtor is ordered to pay, and if he refuses, he is imprisoned. If the claimant cannot prove it, and they differ, the claimant's word is taken in four specific matters (the details of which are in other sources) and the defendant's word in other matters. The text notes that this procedure, transmitted from al-Khassaf, contradicts the preferred Hanafi position, but there is consensus that if the debtor requests, the judge asks the claimant about the debtor's assets. The passage then covers the minimum amount for imprisonment (even a daaniq, one-sixth of a dirham), and the ruling on a debtor who offers to sell his goods or has real estate: he is given three days or imprisoned to sell. If a debtor refuses a loan that could pay the debt, he is unjust. The text expands on what constitutes 'price' (thaman), including wages, amounts owed after rescission, salam capital, etc., and distinguishes between debts that are a substitute for property (badal mal) – for which imprisonment is allowed – and those that are not, such as dower and guarantee, where some later fatwas do not permit imprisonment. The passage also discusses imprisonment for a usurped (maghsubah) tangible thing, citing Tahdhib al-Qalanisi, which includes imprisonment for every debt that is a substitute for property, and for any contractually undertaken obligation. The key rule is that imprisonment is for debts that are substitutes for property, not for personal obligations like dower or guarantees.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses, that proves a claim in court.
إقرار (iqrar): Confession by the debtor that he owes the debt, which establishes the right without further proof.
بدل مال (badal mal): A debt that is a substitute for property, such as the price of a sold item or the principal of a loan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following debts does NOT warrant imprisonment?
A) Price of a sale (thaman al-mabi')
B) Loan principal (badl al-qard)
C) Dower (mahr)
D) Salam capital after revocation

Key Takeaway
Imprisonment for debt in Hanafi fiqh is a coercive measure strictly limited to debts that are substitutes for property (badal mal), such as the price of a sale or loan principal, and is not allowed for personal obligations like dower or guarantee.
