Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
10 min
Evidence of Insolvency: Witnesses and Procedure
Commentary on the evidence needed to prove insolvency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the number and quality of witnesses required to prove insolvency and distinguish between dispute and non-dispute situations.

Core Idea
One just witness suffices to prove insolvency in non-dispute cases; two are precautionary, and full evidence is needed in disputes.

This lesson dives into the evidentiary requirements for establishing a debtor's insolvency. The commentary clarifies that in a non-contentious setting, a single just witness (adl) is enough, but when the debtor and creditor are in dispute, formal evidence (bayyinah) with two witnesses is necessary. It also addresses the condition of the witness's justice and how to testify.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule on witnesses
The passage states that one just witness suffices to prove insolvency in non-dispute situations. Two witnesses are more precautionary. Note that formal wording of testimony is not required.
This is a relaxation from the usual two-witness rule.
2
Distinguish between dispute and non-dispute
If the debtor claims insolvency and the creditor denies it, it becomes a dispute. In that case, one witness is insufficient; full bayyinah (two male witnesses or one male and two females) is required.
This distinction is crucial for practice.
3
Examine the conditions of the witness
The witness must be just (adl). A concealed person (mastur) is like a just one. A corrupt person (fasiq) is not accepted. There is a minor ikhtilaf: al-Zayla'i objected that requiring justice is not from the madhhab, but the commentary defends it.
The requirement of justice is affirmed by later sources.
4
Describe the manner of testimony
The witness should describe the debtor's situation: his expenses, clothing, and overall tight condition, and that they have examined him privately and publicly. The creditor need not be present; if absent, the judge releases with a surety.
The testimony is about external signs, not a declaration of insolvency.

كتاب القضاء ثم يسأل عنه فإن لم يظهر له مال خلاه ولم يحل بينه وبين غرمائه ورد البينة على إفلاسه قبل كما وفي رواية الحسن بأربعة وفي رواية الطحاوي بنصف الحول والصحيح ما ذكره المصنف في البزازية، فلو رأى وقحاً حبسه ستة أشهر ثم سأل عنه، وهذا إذا كان حاله مشكلاً عند القاضي وإلا عمل بما ظهر له. قوله : (ثم يسأل عنه أي يسأل القاضي عن المحبوس بعد حبسه بقدر ما يراه من جيرانه، فإن قامت بينة على إعساره أطلقه ولا يحتاج إلى لفظ الشهادة وشرطه في الصغرى. والعدل الواحد يكفي والاثنان أحوط وكيفيته أن يقول المخبر أن حاله حال المعسرين في نفقته وكسوته وحالته ضيقة وقد اختبرنا حاله في السر والعلانية ولا يشترط لسماعها نظر لقوله في الخلاصة والبزازية : وإنما يسأل من العدول فليس ذكرها من كلامه ثم اعلم أن قولهم إن الواحد يكفي مقيد بما إذا لم يكن الحال حال منازعة، أما إذا كان حال منازعة بأن ادعى المطلوب أنه معسر وادعى الطالب أنه فلا بد من إقامة البينة؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج معزياً إلى النهاية. وظاهر إطلاق المصنف أن الحبس أولاً ثم السؤال في حق كل أحد ولكن في البزازية : إن كان أمر المديون ظاهراً عند الناس فالقاضي يقبل بينة الاعسار ويخليه قبل المدة التي يذكرها، وإن كان أمره مشكلاً هل يقبل البينة قبل الحبس؟ فيه روايتان ا هـ . وفي الملتقط قال أبو حنيفة : لا أسأل عن المعسر وأحبسه شهرين أو ثلاثة ثم أسأل عنه إلا إذا كان معروفاً بالعسرة فلا أحبسه ا هـ. وفيه أيضاً : ولو معسراً عليه دين وله على موسر دين يعلم به القاضي يحبس المعسر حتى يطالب الموسر، فإذا طالبه وحبس الموسر أطلق المعسر ا هـ . وفي البزازية : ولو للمحبوس مال في بلد آخر يطلقه بكفيل وإن علم القاضي عسرته لكن له مال على آخر يتقاضى غريمه فإن حبس غريمه الموسر يحبسه ا هـ. وظاهر كلامهم أن القاضي لا يحبس المديون إذا علم أن له مالاً غائباً أو محبوساً موسراً وأنه يطلقه إذا علم بأحدهما قوله : (فإن لم يظهر له مال خلاه) أي أطلقه من الحبس لأن عسرته ثبتت عنده فاستحق النظرة إلى الميسرة للآية فحبسه بعده يكون ظلماً، وظاهره أنه يطلقه بلا كفيل - قلت ـ إلا في مال اليتيم لما في البزازية : ولو للميت على رجل دين وله ورثة صغار وكبار لا يطلقه من الحبس قبل الاستيثاق بكفيل للصغار ا هـ. وقدمنا أنه يطلقه بكفيل إذا كان رب الدين غائباً، وينبغي أن يكون مال الوقف كمال اليتيم فلا يطلقه القاضي إلا بكفيل فهي ثلاثة مواضع مستثناة. والكلام في إطلاقه جبراً على رب الدين، فلو أطلقه رب الدين من غير بينة على إفلاسه ورضي المحبوس جاز ولا يتوقف على حضور القاضي - كما في البزازية - إلا في مال اليتيم فلا يطلقه الوصي. وفي وصايا القنية : حبس الوصي غريماً بدين الصبي ليس له أن يطلقه قبل قضائه إذا كان موسراً؛ وإن رأى أن يأخذ منه كفيلاً أو يطلقه فله ذلك . ثم رقم آخر : إذا كان معسراً جاز إطلاقه ا هـ. فتحرر أن المعسر يجوز إطلاقه اتفاقاً وفي الموسر خلاف وقيدنا برضا المحبوس لما في القنية : المحبوس بالدين أقام البينة على إفلاسه فأراد رب الدين أن يطلقه قبل القضاء بإفلاسه وأبى المحبوس أن يخرج حتى يقضي بإفلاسه يجب على القاضي القضاء به حتى لا يعيده رب الدين ثانياً قبل ظهور غناه ا.هـ. وإذا أطلقه بلا بينة فله إعادته إلى الحبس كما في أنفع الوسائل. وأشار بقوله خلاه إلى أنه لا يحبسه مرة أخرى But in al-Bazzaziyyah: If the debtor's matter is apparent to the people, the judge accepts evidence of insolvency and releases him before the period mentioned; if his matter is doubtful, is evidence accepted before imprisonment? There are two narrations. And in al-Multaqat: Abu Hanifa said: I do not ask about the insolvent and I imprison him for two or three months then ask about him, except if he is known to have insolvency, then I do not imprison him. And also: If an insolvent owes a debt and has a claim against a wealthy person, and the judge knows it, he imprisons the insolvent until he sues the wealthy; when he sues and the wealthy is imprisoned, he releases the insolvent. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If the imprisoned has wealth in another land, he releases him with a surety, even if the judge knows his insolvency, but he has wealth against another; he sues his debtor; if he imprisons his wealthy debtor, he imprisons him. The apparent meaning of their words is that the judge does not imprison the debtor if he knows he has absent wealth or an imprisoned wealthy debtor, and he releases him if he knows either. His statement: (then if no wealth appears for him, he releases him) means he releases him from imprisonment because his insolvency is established with him, so he deserves a deferment until ease, due to the verse; imprisoning him after that would be oppression. It is apparent that he releases him without a surety. But I say: except for the property of an orphan, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: If a deceased owes a debt and has minor and adult heirs, he does not release him from imprisonment before securing a surety for the minors. And we have presented that he releases him with a surety if the creditor is absent. And the property of an endowment should be like the property of an orphan; so the judge does not release him except with a surety. So there are three exceptional cases. The discussion is about releasing him forcibly over the creditor. If the creditor releases him without evidence of insolvency and the imprisoned agrees, it is permissible and does not depend on the presence of the judge—as in al-Bazzaziyyah—except for the orphan's property, so the guardian may not release him. And in Wasaaya al-Qunyah: The guardian's imprisoning a debtor for the debt of a minor: he may not release him before payment if he is wealthy; if he sees to take a surety from him or release him, that is his choice. Then another annotation: If he is insolvent, it is permissible to release him. So it is established that the insolvent is permissible to release unanimously, and regarding the wealthy there is disagreement, and we restrict it with the consent of the imprisoned because of al-Qunyah: The imprisoned for debt established evidence of his insolvency, and the creditor wanted to release him before the judgment of insolvency, and the imprisoned refused to leave until judgment of insolvency is passed; the judge must pass judgment so that the creditor does not re-imprison him again before his wealth appears. If he releases him without evidence, he may re-imprison him, as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il. And he indicated by his word 'yukhalleehi' that he does not imprison him again for the first or other debt until his creditor proves his wealth, as in al-Bazzaziyyah: The judge released the imprisoned for his insolvency, then another claimed property against him and claimed that he does not imprison him until his ease is known and the appearance of no wealth by testimony that he has no wealth. And he said: wealthy. There is a narration in the Book of Suretyship: it is accepted; and in another narration: not accepted; the author of the book explicitly stated it at the end of the chapter, and the general body of shaykhs used to give fatwa according to it, and it is the correct one. If the defendant brings evidence after imprisonment before this time we mentioned of absence, and they testify before the judge about that, the author of the book said: The judge accepts that and removes him from imprisonment and declares him insolvent. The rest of it is in his statement: (In al-Bazzaziyyah: If the imprisoned has wealth in another land...) repeated with what he presented earlier in the statement before this. His statement: (When he knows that he has absent wealth or an imprisoned wealthy debtor) Al-Ramli said: The pronoun refers to the debtor, and 'wealthy' is a description of 'imprisoned'; the meaning is that the insolvent debtor, if he has absent wealth or has a debtor who is imprisoned and his debtor is wealthy, the judge does not imprison him. His statement: (And if he releases him without evidence, he may re-imprison him, as in Anfa' al-Wasa'il) Al-Nahr said: I did not find it therein, and it must be interpreted as when a lawsuit occurs without evidence. As for when it does not occur, he may not re-imprison him because this matter is subject to his judgment, and you know that inquiry is not obligatory but is a precaution. If his judgment dictates his release, he may not re-imprison him after that. This is supported by what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: He released...
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (expansion probe) on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the procedure for a judge when dealing with a debtor who claims insolvency. The base ruling states that the judge first imprisons the debtor, then inquires about his financial status; if no wealth is found, he releases him. The commentary clarifies and expands on this with multiple Hanafi sources.

The passage begins by noting different narrations on the period of imprisonment before inquiry: one narration from Imam al-Hasan says four months, from al-Tahawi half a year, but the correct view as per al-Bazzaziyyah is that the judge may use his discretion. If the judge sees fit to release after a day, he may. Al-Muhit states the judge may inquire before a month. Al-Sadr al-Shahid differentiates based on the debtor's character: if the debtor is soft or has a family, one month; if shameless, six months. This applies when the judge is uncertain; otherwise, he acts on what is clear.

The commentary then explains the inquiry: the judge asks neighbors about the debtor's condition. If they testify to his insolvency, he releases him without formal testimony wording. One just (adl) witness suffices, but two are preferable. The witness must describe the debtor's poverty after examining him privately and publicly. The creditor need not be present; if absent, the judge releases with a surety. The witness must be just; a concealed person (mastur) is like a just one, but a corrupt person (fasiq) is not accepted. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Zayla'i objected that requiring justice is not transmitted from the madhhab, but the commentary defends it with citations from al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah.

Further details: The rule that one witness suffices is restricted to non-dispute situations. If the debtor claims insolvency and the creditor disputes, full evidence (bayyinah) is required. The general application of the matn is that imprisonment precedes inquiry for everyone, but al-Bazzaziyyah states that if the debtor's condition is publicly known, the judge may accept evidence and release before the typical period. If doubtful, there are two narrations on whether evidence is accepted before imprisonment.

Abu Hanifa's own view (from al-Multaqat) is that he does not inquire about insolvency but imprisons for two or three months then inquires, unless the debtor is known to be insolvent, in which case no imprisonment. Also, if an insolvent debtor has a claim against a wealthy person, the judge imprisons the insolvent until he pursues that claim, then releases him when the wealthy is imprisoned.

The passage then addresses the release: if no wealth appears, the judge releases him because his insolvency is established, and further imprisonment would be oppression (citing the Quranic verse 'until ease' [2:280]). The default is release without surety, but there are three exceptions: (1) when the creditor is absent (release with surety), (2) when the debt is for an orphan's property (release only with surety), and (3) when the debt is for a waqf (endowment), analogized to orphan's property.

If the creditor himself releases the debtor without evidence and the debtor agrees, it is valid without the judge, except for orphan's property where the guardian cannot release. For a wealthy debtor, there is disagreement; the commentary restricts it to the debtor's consent.

Finally, re-imprisonment: If the judge releases without evidence, he may re-imprison if a new lawsuit arises without evidence. However, if no lawsuit occurs, he cannot re-imprison because the release was based on his judgment. The passage ends with a discussion of the correct narration regarding testimony of insolvency after imprisonment.

Throughout, the commentary relies on authoritative Hanafi texts: al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Muhit, al-Nihayah, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Multaqat, among others, demonstrating the depth of the Hanafi tradition in procedural matters of insolvency.

Key Terms
عدل (adl): Just person; one whose testimony is acceptable.
مستور (mastur): A person whose condition is concealed (not known for corruption or scrupulousness); treated like an adl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation does one just witness suffice to prove insolvency according to this passage?
A) When the debtor is known to be honest.
B) When the creditor is present and does not dispute.
C) When the debtor claims insolvency and the creditor agrees.
D) When there is no dispute between debtor and creditor.

Key Takeaway
The number of witnesses for insolvency depends on context: one just witness in non-dispute, full evidence in dispute.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
10 min
Exceptions to Release Without Surety and Rules on Re-imprisonment
Commentary on exceptions and re-imprisonment rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three exceptions where a releasing judge must take a surety, and state the Hanafi rule on re-imprisonment after release for insolvency.

Core Idea
Release without surety is default, but three exceptions require a surety: absent creditor, orphan's property, and waqf; re-imprisonment is allowed only with new evidence or lawsuit.

This lesson covers two important details: when a judge must take a surety upon releasing an insolvent debtor, and when he may re-imprison a debtor after release. The default is release without surety, but exceptions protect vulnerable property (orphans, waqf) and absent creditors. The rule on re-imprisonment balances finality with the creditor's right to later prove the debtor's wealth.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule
The passage states that if no wealth appears, the judge releases the debtor without surety. This is based on the Quranic command of respite.
Imprisonment after proof of insolvency is oppression.
2
Identify the three exceptions
List the three cases where a surety is required: (1) when the creditor is absent, (2) when the debt involves orphan's property, and (3) when the debt involves waqf (endowment) property.
These are derived from al-Bazzaziyyah and analogy.
3
Examine the rule on re-imprisonment
If the judge releases without evidence (based on his own judgment), he may re-imprison the debtor if a new lawsuit arises without evidence. However, if no lawsuit occurs, he cannot re-imprison because the release was based on his discretion.
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether testimony of insolvency after imprisonment is accepted; the relied-upon view is that it is accepted.
4
Note the condition for re-imprisonment
After release due to insolvency, the judge cannot re-imprison the debtor for the same debt unless the creditor proves that the debtor has become wealthy. Re-imprisonment for a different debt requires a new lawsuit.
This protects the debtor's finality of release.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (expansion probe) on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the procedure for a judge when dealing with a debtor who claims insolvency. The base ruling states that the judge first imprisons the debtor, then inquires about his financial status; if no wealth is found, he releases him. The commentary clarifies and expands on this with multiple Hanafi sources.

The passage begins by noting different narrations on the period of imprisonment before inquiry: one narration from Imam al-Hasan says four months, from al-Tahawi half a year, but the correct view as per al-Bazzaziyyah is that the judge may use his discretion. If the judge sees fit to release after a day, he may. Al-Muhit states the judge may inquire before a month. Al-Sadr al-Shahid differentiates based on the debtor's character: if the debtor is soft or has a family, one month; if shameless, six months. This applies when the judge is uncertain; otherwise, he acts on what is clear.

The commentary then explains the inquiry: the judge asks neighbors about the debtor's condition. If they testify to his insolvency, he releases him without formal testimony wording. One just (adl) witness suffices, but two are preferable. The witness must describe the debtor's poverty after examining him privately and publicly. The creditor need not be present; if absent, the judge releases with a surety. The witness must be just; a concealed person (mastur) is like a just one, but a corrupt person (fasiq) is not accepted. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Zayla'i objected that requiring justice is not transmitted from the madhhab, but the commentary defends it with citations from al-Khulasah and al-Bazzaziyyah.

Further details: The rule that one witness suffices is restricted to non-dispute situations. If the debtor claims insolvency and the creditor disputes, full evidence (bayyinah) is required. The general application of the matn is that imprisonment precedes inquiry for everyone, but al-Bazzaziyyah states that if the debtor's condition is publicly known, the judge may accept evidence and release before the typical period. If doubtful, there are two narrations on whether evidence is accepted before imprisonment.

Abu Hanifa's own view (from al-Multaqat) is that he does not inquire about insolvency but imprisons for two or three months then inquires, unless the debtor is known to be insolvent, in which case no imprisonment. Also, if an insolvent debtor has a claim against a wealthy person, the judge imprisons the insolvent until he pursues that claim, then releases him when the wealthy is imprisoned.

The passage then addresses the release: if no wealth appears, the judge releases him because his insolvency is established, and further imprisonment would be oppression (citing the Quranic verse 'until ease' [2:280]). The default is release without surety, but there are three exceptions: (1) when the creditor is absent (release with surety), (2) when the debt is for an orphan's property (release only with surety), and (3) when the debt is for a waqf (endowment), analogized to orphan's property.

If the creditor himself releases the debtor without evidence and the debtor agrees, it is valid without the judge, except for orphan's property where the guardian cannot release. For a wealthy debtor, there is disagreement; the commentary restricts it to the debtor's consent.

Finally, re-imprisonment: If the judge releases without evidence, he may re-imprison if a new lawsuit arises without evidence. However, if no lawsuit occurs, he cannot re-imprison because the release was based on his judgment. The passage ends with a discussion of the correct narration regarding testimony of insolvency after imprisonment.

Throughout, the commentary relies on authoritative Hanafi texts: al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Muhit, al-Nihayah, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, and al-Multaqat, among others, demonstrating the depth of the Hanafi tradition in procedural matters of insolvency.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafeel): Surety; a person who guarantees the debtor's appearance or payment.
وقف (waqf): Endowment; property dedicated for religious or charitable purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following situations must a judge take a surety when releasing an insolvent debtor?
A) When the creditor is present and agrees to the release.
B) When the debtor is known to be honest.
C) When the debt involves orphan's property.
D) When the debtor has a family.

Key Takeaway
Release without surety is the default, but three exceptions (absent creditor, orphan's property, waqf) require a surety; re-imprisonment after release is limited.
